            <meta name="dc.title" content="Comparison of the Informed Health Choices Key Concepts Framework to other frameworks relevant to teaching and learning how to think critically about health claims and choices: a systematic review">
            <meta name="dc.description" content=" Background: The Informed Health Choices (IHC) Key Concepts are principles for evaluating the trustworthiness of claims about treatment effects. The Key Concepts provide a framework for developing learning-resources to help people use the concepts when treatment claims are made, and when they make health choices. 
 Objective: To compare the framework provided by the IHC Key Concepts to other frameworks intended to promote critical thinking about treatment (intervention) claims and choices. 
 Methods: We identified relevant frameworks from reviews of frameworks, searching Google Scholar, citation searches, and contact with key informants. We included frameworks intended to provide a structure for teaching or learning to think critically about the basis for claims, evidence used to support claims, or informed choices. For a framework to be included, there had to be a description of its purpose; a list of concepts, competences, or dispositions; and definitions of key terms. We made independent assessments of framework eligibility and extracted data for each included framework using standardised forms. 
 Results: Twenty-two frameworks met our inclusion criteria. The purpose of the IHC Framework is similar to that of two frameworks for critical thinking and somewhat similar to that of a framework for evidence-based practice. Those frameworks have broader scopes than the IHC Framework. An important limitation of broad frameworks is that they do not provide an adequate basis (concepts) for deciding which claims to believe and what to do. There was at most some overlap between the concepts, competences, and dispositions in each of the 22 included frameworks and those in the IHC Framework. 
 Conclusions: The IHC Key Concepts Framework appears to be unique.&amp;nbsp; Our review has shown how it and other frameworks can be improved by taking account of the ways in which other related frameworks have been developed, evaluated, and made useful.">

            <meta name="citation_abstract" content=" Background: The Informed Health Choices (IHC) Key Concepts are principles for evaluating the trustworthiness of claims about treatment effects. The Key Concepts provide a framework for developing learning-resources to help people use the concepts when treatment claims are made, and when they make health choices. 
 Objective: To compare the framework provided by the IHC Key Concepts to other frameworks intended to promote critical thinking about treatment (intervention) claims and choices. 
 Methods: We identified relevant frameworks from reviews of frameworks, searching Google Scholar, citation searches, and contact with key informants. We included frameworks intended to provide a structure for teaching or learning to think critically about the basis for claims, evidence used to support claims, or informed choices. For a framework to be included, there had to be a description of its purpose; a list of concepts, competences, or dispositions; and definitions of key terms. We made independent assessments of framework eligibility and extracted data for each included framework using standardised forms. 
 Results: Twenty-two frameworks met our inclusion criteria. The purpose of the IHC Framework is similar to that of two frameworks for critical thinking and somewhat similar to that of a framework for evidence-based practice. Those frameworks have broader scopes than the IHC Framework. An important limitation of broad frameworks is that they do not provide an adequate basis (concepts) for deciding which claims to believe and what to do. There was at most some overlap between the concepts, competences, and dispositions in each of the 22 included frameworks and those in the IHC Framework. 
 Conclusions: The IHC Key Concepts Framework appears to be unique.&amp;nbsp; Our review has shown how it and other frameworks can be improved by taking account of the ways in which other related frameworks have been developed, evaluated, and made useful.">
            <meta name="citation_description" content=" Background: The Informed Health Choices (IHC) Key Concepts are principles for evaluating the trustworthiness of claims about treatment effects. The Key Concepts provide a framework for developing learning-resources to help people use the concepts when treatment claims are made, and when they make health choices. 
 Objective: To compare the framework provided by the IHC Key Concepts to other frameworks intended to promote critical thinking about treatment (intervention) claims and choices. 
 Methods: We identified relevant frameworks from reviews of frameworks, searching Google Scholar, citation searches, and contact with key informants. We included frameworks intended to provide a structure for teaching or learning to think critically about the basis for claims, evidence used to support claims, or informed choices. For a framework to be included, there had to be a description of its purpose; a list of concepts, competences, or dispositions; and definitions of key terms. We made independent assessments of framework eligibility and extracted data for each included framework using standardised forms. 
 Results: Twenty-two frameworks met our inclusion criteria. The purpose of the IHC Framework is similar to that of two frameworks for critical thinking and somewhat similar to that of a framework for evidence-based practice. Those frameworks have broader scopes than the IHC Framework. An important limitation of broad frameworks is that they do not provide an adequate basis (concepts) for deciding which claims to believe and what to do. There was at most some overlap between the concepts, competences, and dispositions in each of the 22 included frameworks and those in the IHC Framework. 
 Conclusions: The IHC Key Concepts Framework appears to be unique.&amp;nbsp; Our review has shown how it and other frameworks can be improved by taking account of the ways in which other related frameworks have been developed, evaluated, and made useful.">

{
  "@context": "https://schema.org",
  "@type": "ScholarlyArticle",
  "mainEntityOfPage": {
    "@type": "WebPage",
    "@id": "https://f1000research.com/articles/9-164"
  },
  "headline": "Comparison of the Informed Health Choices Key Concepts Framework to other frameworks relevant to teaching...",
  "datePublished": "2020-03-05T11:41:39",
  "dateModified": "2020-03-05T11:41:39",
  "author": [
    {
      "@type": "Person",
      "name": "Andrew D. Oxman"
    },    {
      "@type": "Person",
      "name": "Laura Mart&iacute;nez Garc&iacute;a"
    }  ],
  "publisher": {
    "@type": "Organization",
    "name": "F1000Research",
    "logo": {
      "@type": "ImageObject",
      "url": "https://f1000research.com/img/AMP/F1000Research_image.png",
      "height": 480,
      "width": 60
    }
  },
  "image": {
    "@type": "ImageObject",
    "url": "https://f1000research.com/img/AMP/F1000Research_image.png",
    "height": 1200,
    "width": 150
  },
  "description": "Background: The Informed Health Choices (IHC) Key Concepts are principles for evaluating the trustworthiness of claims about treatment effects. The Key Concepts provide a framework for developing learning-resources to help people use the concepts when treatment claims are made, and when they make health choices.
Objective: To compare the framework provided by the IHC Key Concepts to other frameworks intended to promote critical thinking about treatment (intervention) claims and choices.
Methods: We identified relevant frameworks from reviews of frameworks, searching Google Scholar, citation searches, and contact with key informants. We included frameworks intended to provide a structure for teaching or learning to think critically about the basis for claims, evidence used to support claims, or informed choices. For a framework to be included, there had to be a description of its purpose; a list of concepts, competences, or dispositions; and definitions of key terms. We made independent assessments of framework eligibility and extracted data for each included framework using standardised forms.
Results: Twenty-two frameworks met our inclusion criteria. The purpose of the IHC Framework is similar to that of two frameworks for critical thinking and somewhat similar to that of a framework for evidence-based practice. Those frameworks have broader scopes than the IHC Framework. An important limitation of broad frameworks is that they do not provide an adequate basis (concepts) for deciding which claims to believe and what to do. There was at most some overlap between the concepts, competences, and dispositions in each of the 22 included frameworks and those in the IHC Framework.
Conclusions: The IHC Key Concepts Framework appears to be unique.  Our review has shown how it and other frameworks can be improved by taking account of the ways in which other related frameworks have been developed, evaluated, and made useful."
}

Oxman</span></a><a href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-5608-5061" target=_blank id=mauthor-orcid-0><span class=orcid-logo-for-author-list></span></a><div class="mdl-tooltip mdl-tooltip--wider" for=mauthor-orcid-0><span class=orcid-logo-for-author-list></span> https://orcid.org/0000-0002-5608-5061</div><sup>1,2</sup>,&nbsp;</span><span class="">Laura Mart&iacute;nez Garc&iacute;a<a href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-0126-8706" target=_blank id=mauthor-orcid-1><span class=orcid-logo-for-author-list></span></a><div class="mdl-tooltip mdl-tooltip--wider" for=mauthor-orcid-1><span class=orcid-logo-for-author-list></span> https://orcid.org/0000-0003-0126-8706</div><sup>3,4</sup></span></div> </div> <div class=f1r-article-mobile> <div class=article-pubinfo-mobile> PUBLISHED 05 Mar 2020 </div> </div> <span class=Z3988 title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft_id=info:doi/10.12688%2Ff1000research.21858.1"></span> <div class=f1r-article-desk> <div class="contracted-details first"> <a href="#" class="contracted-details-label author-affiliations"><span class=contracted></span>Author details</a> <a href="#" class=section-title>Author details</a> <span class="f1r-icon icon-14_more_small section-control"></span> <span class="f1r-icon icon-10_less_small section-control"></span> <div class="expanded-details affiliations is-hidden"> <sup>1</sup> Centre for Informed Health Choices, Norwegian Institute of Public Health, Oslo, Norway<br/> <sup>2</sup> University of Oslo, Oslo, Norway<br/> <sup>3</sup> Iberoamerican Cochrane Centre, Sant Pau Biomedical Research Institute, Barcelona, Spain<br/> <sup>4</sup> CIBER of Epidemiology and Public Health, Barcelona, Spain<br/> <p> <div class=margin-bottom> Andrew D. Oxman <br/> <span>Roles: </span> Conceptualization, Data Curation, Formal Analysis, Investigation, Methodology, Project Administration, Writing  Original Draft Preparation, Writing  Review & Editing </div> <div class=margin-bottom> Laura Mart&iacute;nez Garc&iacute;a <br/> <span>Roles: </span> Data Curation, Formal Analysis, Investigation, Writing  Review & Editing </div> </p> </div> </div> </div> <div class=f1r-article-mobile> <div class="article-page-section-box margin-bottom-40 research-layout"> <span class=box-title> <span class="f1r-icon icon-85_peer_review"></span> OPEN PEER REVIEW </span> <div class="status-row referee-reports-container"> REVIEWER STATUS <span class=status-icons> <em>AWAITING PEER REVIEW</em> </span> </div> </div> </div> <div class="f1r-article-desk padding-top"> <div class=article-collection-wrapper> <div class="o-media o-media--sm o-media--center"> <div class=o-media__img> <a href="https://f1000research.com/gateways/scipolresearch" title="Open Gateway"><img src="https://f1000researchdata.s3.amazonaws.com/thumbnails/77c4a20e-5a57-4e85-8d9d-6a7d9a6e4fb3_collection.thumbnail"></a> </div> <div class=o-media__body> <p class="u-mt--0 u-mb--0">This article is included in the <a href="https://f1000research.com/gateways/scipolresearch">Science Policy Research</a> gateway.</p> </div> </div> </div> </div> </div> <h2 class="article-headings article-page-abstract" id=anchor-abstract> <span class="f1r-article-mobile-inline abstract-heading-border"></span> Abstract </h2> <div class="article-abstract article-page-general-text-mobile research-layout"> <div class="abstract-text is-expanded"> <b>Background:</b> The Informed Health Choices (IHC) Key Concepts are principles for evaluating the trustworthiness of claims about treatment effects. The Key Concepts provide a framework for developing learning-resources to help people use the concepts when treatment claims are made, and when they make health choices.<br/> <b>Objective: </b>To compare the framework provided by the IHC Key Concepts to other frameworks intended to promote critical thinking about treatment (intervention) claims and choices.<br/> <b>Methods:</b> We identified relevant frameworks from reviews of frameworks, searching Google Scholar, citation searches, and contact with key informants. We included frameworks intended to provide a structure for teaching or learning to think critically about the basis for claims, evidence used to support claims, or informed choices. For a framework to be included, there had to be a description of its purpose; a list of concepts, competences, or dispositions; and definitions of key terms. We made independent assessments of framework eligibility and extracted data for each included framework using standardised forms.<br/> <b>Results: </b>Twenty-two frameworks met our inclusion criteria. The purpose of the IHC Framework is similar to that of two frameworks for critical thinking and somewhat similar to that of a framework for evidence-based practice. Those frameworks have broader scopes than the IHC Framework. An important limitation of broad frameworks is that they do not provide an adequate basis (concepts) for deciding which claims to believe and what to do. There was at most some overlap between the concepts, competences, and dispositions in each of the 22 included frameworks and those in the IHC Framework.<br/> <b>Conclusions: </b>The IHC Key Concepts Framework appears to be unique.&nbsp; Our review has shown how it and other frameworks can be improved by taking account of the ways in which other related frameworks have been developed, evaluated, and made useful. </div> <div class=abstract-for-mobile> <div class="margin-top-30 padding-bottom-30 research-layout is-centered"> <button class="primary orange-text white-bg bigger-text abstract-expand-button-mobile with-border show" style="display: none;"> READ ALL <span class="f1r-icon icon-14_more_small orange vmiddle big"></span> </button> <button class="primary orange-text white-bg bigger-text abstract-expand-button-mobile with-border hide"> READ LESS <span class="f1r-icon icon-10_less_small orange vmiddle big"></span> </button> </div> </div> </div> <div class=clearfix></div> <div class="article-context no-divider"> <div class="article-abstract article-page-general-text-mobile research-layout generated-article-body"> <h2 class=main-title>Keywords</h2> <p class="u-mb--0 u-pb--2"> critical thinking, evidence-informed decision-making, evidence-based practice, evidence informed decision-making, argumentation, causal inference, cognitive biases, epistemic cognition, health literacy, logical fallacies, meta-cognition, scientific thinking, frameworks, models, concepts, competences, concepts </p> </div> </div> <div class=article-information> <span class="info-separation padding-bottom"> <div id=corresponding-author-icon class="email-icon float-left"> <span class="f1r-icon icon-6_email orange"></span> <div id=corresponding-author-window class="margin-top-20 popup-window-wrapper is-hidden"> <div class="popup-window corresponding-authors-popup"> <div class=corresponding-author-container> <div class="popup-window-title small">Corresponding Author(s)</div> <div class=authors> Andrew D. Oxman (<a href="mailto:AndrewDavid.Oxman@fhi.no">AndrewDavid.Oxman@fhi.no</a>) </div> </div> <div class="margin-top margin-bottom float-left"> <button id=close-popup-window class=general-white-orange-button>Close</button> </div> </div> </div> </div> <span class="icon-text float-left" data-test-id=box-corresponding-author> <b>Corresponding author:</b> Andrew D. Oxman </span> <div class=clearfix></div> </span> <span class="info-separation padding-bottom competing-interests-display"> <span class=competing-interests-title>Competing interests:</span> AO has helped to coordinate the development of the IHC Key Concepts since 2013 and currently has primary responsibility for further development of that framework. Both AO and LMG are members of the IHC Network. AO coordinated the GRADE Working Group from 2000 to 2009; he was a member of the Evidence-Based Medicine Working Group, which prepared the users guides to the medical literature, and co-author of some of the guides; and he was a co-author of the Cochrane Collaborations tool for assessing risk of bias in randomised trials. </span> <div class="info-separation padding-bottom grant-information-display"> <span class=grant-information-title>Grant information:</span> The IHC Key Concepts were developed as part of the Informed Health Choices Project, which was funded by the Research Council of Norway (project no: 220603). This review was prepared as part of a project to develop and evaluate IHC learning resources for secondary schools in East Africa, which is funded by the Research Council of Norway (project no: 284683). Laura Martnez Garca has a Miguel Servet research contract from the Institute of Health Carlos III [CP18/00007]. <br/> <i>The funders had no role in study design, data collection and analysis, decision to publish, or preparation of the manuscript.</i> </div> <span class="f1r-article-desk info-separation padding-bottom"> <span class="copywrite-icon float-left"> <span class="f1r-icon icon-100_open_access"></span> </span> <span class="icon-text float-left" data-test-id=box-copyright-text> <b>Copyright:</b>&nbsp;  2020 Oxman AD and Garca LM. This is an open access article distributed under the terms of the <a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/" target=_blank data-test-id=box-licence-link>Creative Commons Attribution License</a>, which permits unrestricted use, distribution, and reproduction in any medium, provided the original work is properly cited. </span> <div class=clearfix></div> </span> <span class="info-separation padding-bottom" data-test-id=box-how-to-cite> <b>How to cite:</b> <span class="article-title-and-info in-article-box"> Oxman AD and Garca LM. Comparison of the Informed Health Choices Key Concepts Framework to other frameworks relevant to teaching and learning how to think critically about health claims and choices: a systematic review [version 1; peer review: awaiting peer review]</span>. <i>F1000Research</i> 2020, <b>9</b>:164 (<a href="https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.21858.1" target=_blank>https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.21858.1</a>) </span> <span class=info-separation data-test-id=box-first-published><b>First published:</b> 05 Mar 2020, <b>9</b>:164 (<a href="https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.21858.1" target=_blank>https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.21858.1</a>)</span> <span class=info-separation data-test-id=box-latest-published><b>Latest published:</b> 05 Mar 2020, <b>9</b>:164 (<a href="https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.21858.1" target=_blank>https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.21858.1</a>)</span> </div> <div class=clearfix></div> <div id=article-context class=article-context> <div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d3365e189>Introduction</h2><div class=section><a name=d3365e192 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>The Informed Health Choices (IHC) Key Concepts Framework</h3><p class="" id=d3365e197>Claims about what people can do to improve or protect their health (treatments) are ubiquitous. They are found in the mass media, advertisements, and everyday personal communication. Some are based on trustworthy evidence. Many are not, and many people have difficulties determining which claims to believe and act on. Acting on untrustworthy claims and not acting on ones that are trustworthy can result in unnecessary suffering and waste.</p><p class="" id=d3365e200>In response to these challenges, we developed the Informed Health Choices (IHC) Key Concepts as the first step in the IHC project<sup><a href="#ref-1">1</a><a href="#ref-4">4</a></sup>. The aim of the IHC project is to help people, particularly primary and secondary school students, learn to assess treatment claims and make informed health choices<sup><a href="#ref-5">5</a></sup>.</p><p class="" id=d3365e214>We use treatment to refer to any intervention or action intended to protect or improve health<sup><a href="#ref-6">6</a></sup>. People in other fields have found the IHC Key Concepts relevant for assessing claims about the effects of other types of interventions<sup><a href="#ref-7">7</a></sup>. This includes agricultural, educational, environmental, management, social welfare, economic, international development, nutrition, policing, and veterinary interventions.</p><p class="" id=d3365e225>The IHC Key Concepts provide a framework for designing curricula, learning resources, and evaluation tools<sup><a href="#ref-5">5</a>,<a href="#ref-8">8</a></sup>. We first published the framework in 2015<sup><a href="#ref-1">1</a></sup> and have continued to update it yearly. The current (2019) framework includes 49 concepts in three groups (<a href="#T1">Table 1</a>), 20 competences in four groups (<a href="#T2">Table 2</a>), and 16 dispositions in four groups (<a href="#T3">Table 3</a>)<sup><a href="#ref-4">4</a></sup>. The concepts are principles for evaluating the trustworthiness of treatment claims and the evidence used to support these, and for making informed choices. The methods used to develop the framework are described elsewhere<sup><a href="#ref-1">1</a>,<a href="#ref-3">3</a></sup>. The framework is a starting point to help teachers, journalists, researchers and other intermediaries to identify and develop resources to help people learn to assess treatment claims and make informed choices.</p><a name=T1 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 1. Overview of the IHC Key Concepts.</h3></div><table xmlns:ali="http://www.niso.org/schemas/ali/1.0/" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" class=article-table frame=below><a name=d3365e272 class=n-a></a><thead><a name=d3365e274 class=n-a></a><tr><a name=d3365e276 class=n-a></a><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e278 class=n-a></a>1. Claims<br class=br> <br class=br><i>Claims about effects that are not supported by</i><br class=br><i>evidence from fair comparisons are not necessarily</i><br class=br><i>wrong, but there is an insufficient basis for</i><br class=br><i>believing them.</i> </th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e296 class=n-a></a>2. Comparisons<br class=br> <br class=br><i>Studies should make fair comparisons, designed</i><br class=br><i>to minimize the risk of systematic errors (biases)</i><br class=br><i>and random errors (the play of chance).</i> </th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e311 class=n-a></a>3. Choices<br class=br> <br class=br><i>What to do depends on judgements</i><br class=br><i>about a problem, the relevance</i><br class=br><i>of the evidence available, and</i><br class=br><i>the balance of expected benefits,</i><br class=br><i>harms, and costs.</i> </th></tr></thead><tbody><a name=d3365e335 class=n-a></a><tr><a name=d3365e337 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e339 class=n-a></a><b>1.1 It should not be assumed that treatments are</b><br class=br><b>safe or effective - or that they are not.</b><br class=br> <br class=br>a)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Treatments can cause harms as well as benefits.<br class=br> b)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Large, dramatic effects are rare.<br class=br> c)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;It is rarely possible to be certain about the effects<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;of treatments.<br class=br> <br class=br> <b>1.2 Seemingly logical assumptions are not a</b><br class=br><b>sufficient basis for claims.</b> <br class=br> <br class=br>a)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Treatment may not be needed.<br class=br> b)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Beliefs alone about how treatments work are<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;not reliable predictors of the presence or size of<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;effects.<br class=br> c)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Assumptions that fair comparisons of treatments<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;in research are not applicable in practice can be<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;misleading.<br class=br> d)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;An outcome may be associated with a treatment<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;but not caused by it.<br class=br> e)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;More data is not necessarily better data.<br class=br> f)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Identifying effects of treatments depends on<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;making comparisons.<br class=br> g)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The results of one study considered in isolation<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;can be misleading.<br class=br> h)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Widely used treatments or those that have been<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;used for decades are not necessarily beneficial<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;or safe.<br class=br> i)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Treatments that are new or technologically<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;impressive may not be better than available<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;alternatives.<br class=br> j)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Increasing the amount of a treatment does not<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;necessarily increase its benefits and may cause<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;harm.<br class=br> k)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Earlier detection of disease is not necessarily<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;better.<br class=br> l)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;It is rarely possible to know in advance who will<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;benefit, who will not, and who will be harmed by<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;using a treatment.<br class=br> <br class=br><b>1.3 Trust in a source alone is not a sufficient basis</b><br class=br><b>for believing a claim.</b><br class=br> <br class=br> a)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Your existing beliefs may be wrong.<br class=br> b)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Competing interests may result in misleading<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;claims.<br class=br> c)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Personal experiences or anecdotes alone are an<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;unreliable basis for most claims.<br class=br> d)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Opinions alone are not a reliable basis for claims.<br class=br> e)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Peer review and publication by a journal do not<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;guarantee that comparisons have been fair.</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e458 class=n-a></a> <b>2.1 Comparisons of treatments should be fair.</b><br class=br> <br class=br>a)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Comparison groups should be as similar as<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;possible.<br class=br> b)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Indirect comparisons of treatments across<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;different studies can be misleading.<br class=br> c)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The people being compared should be<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;cared for similarly apart from the treatments<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;being studied.<br class=br> d)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;If possible, people should not know which<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;of the treatments being compared they are<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;receiving.<br class=br> e)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Outcomes should be assessed in the same<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;way in all the groups being compared.<br class=br> f)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Outcomes should be assessed using<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;methods that have been shown to be<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;reliable.<br class=br> g)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;It is important to assess outcomes in all (or<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;nearly all) the people in a study.<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; h)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Peoples outcomes should be counted in the<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;group to which they were allocated<b>.</b> <br class=br> <br class=br><b>2.2 Syntheses of studies need to be reliable.</b> <br class=br> <br class=br>a)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Reviews of studies comparing treatments<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;should use systematic methods.<br class=br> b)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Failure to consider unpublished results of<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;fair comparisons may result in estimates of<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;effects that are misleading.<br class=br> c)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Treatment claims based on models may be<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;sensitive to underlying assumptions.<br class=br> <br class=br> <b>2.3 Descriptions should clearly reflect the size</b><br class=br><b>of effects and the risk of being misled by the</b><br class=br><b>play of chance.</b> <br class=br> <br class=br>a)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Verbal descriptions of the size of effects<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;alone can be misleading.<br class=br> b)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Relative effects of treatments alone can be<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;misleading.<br class=br> c)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Average differences between treatments can<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;be misleading.<br class=br> d)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Small studies may be misleading.<br class=br> e)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Results for a selected group of people within<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;a study can be misleading.<br class=br> f)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The use of p-values may be misleading;<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;confidence intervals are more informative.<br class=br> g)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Deeming results to be statistically<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;significant or nonsignificant can be<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;misleading.<br class=br> h)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Lack of evidence of a difference is not the<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;same as evidence of no difference.</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e584 class=n-a></a> <b>3.1 Problems and options should</b><br class=br><b>be clear.</b> <br class=br> <br class=br>a)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Be clear about what the problem<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;or goal is and what the options<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;are.<br class=br> <br class=br> <b>3.2 Evidence should be relevant.</b> <br class=br> <br class=br>a)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Attention should focus on all<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;important effects of treatments,<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;and not surrogate outcomes.<br class=br> b)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Fair comparisons of treatments in<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;animals or highly selected groups<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;of people may not be relevant.<br class=br> c)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The treatments compared should<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;be similar to those of interest.<br class=br> d)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;There should not be important<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;differences between the<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;circumstances in which the<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;treatments were compared and<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;those of interest.<br class=br> <br class=br> <b>3.3 Expected advantages should</b><br class=br><b>outweigh expected disadvantages.</b> <br class=br> <br class=br>a)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Weigh the benefits and savings<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;against the harms and costs of<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;acting or not.<br class=br> b)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Consider the baseline risk or the<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;severity of the symptoms when<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;estimating the size of expected<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;effects.<br class=br> c)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Consider how important each<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;advantage and disadvantage is<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;when weighing the pros and cons.<br class=br> d)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Consider how certain you can<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;be about each advantage and<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;disadvantage.<br class=br> e)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Important uncertainties about<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;the effects of treatments should<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;be addressed in further fair<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;comparisons.</td></tr></tbody></table></div><a name=T2 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 2. IHC competences.</h3></div><table xmlns:ali="http://www.niso.org/schemas/ali/1.0/" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" class=article-table frame=below><a name=d3365e704 class=n-a></a><tbody><a name=d3365e706 class=n-a></a><tr><a name=d3365e708 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e709 class=n-a></a><b>Goal</b> <br class=br> <br class=br>To enable people to make good decisions<a href="#TFN50">*</a> about which claims to believe about the effects of things they can do for their health,<br class=br>the health of others or for other reasons, and about what to do to achieve their goals.<br class=br> <br class=br> <b>Competences</b> <br class=br> <br class=br>To achieve this goal, people should be able to:<br class=br> <br class=br><b>1.</b>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<b>Recognise when a claim has an untrustworthy basis by:</b> <br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;a)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;recognising claims about the effects of treatments<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;b)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;questioning the basis for treatment claims<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;c)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;thinking carefully about treatment claims before believing them<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;d)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;recognising when a treatment claim is relevant and important, and warrants reflection<br class=br> <br class=br><b>2.</b>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<b>Recognise when evidence used to support a treatment claim is trustworthy or untrustworthy by:</b> <br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;a)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;recognising the assumptions, evidence and reasoning behind treatment claims<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;b)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;recognising unfair treatment comparisons<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;c)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;recognising unreliable summaries of treatment comparisons<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;d)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;recognising when a statistical model and its assumptions are used to support a treatment claim<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;e)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;recognising misleading ways of presenting treatment effects<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;f)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;understanding how systematic errors (the risk of bias), random errors (the play of chance), and the relevance<br class=br> &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;(applicability) of treatment comparisons can affect the degree of confidence in estimates of treatment effects<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;g)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;understanding the extent to which evidence does or does not support a treatment claim<br class=br> <br class=br><b>3.</b>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<b>Make well-informed decisions about treatments by:</b> <br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;a)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;being aware of cognitive biases when making decisions<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;b)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;clarifying and understanding the problem, options, and goals when making a decision<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;c)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;recognising when decisions have irreversible consequences<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;d)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;judging the relevance of evidence used to inform decisions about treatments<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;e)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;weighing the advantages and disadvantages of treatments, taking into account the size of treatment effects, how<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;important each outcome is, the costs, and the certainty of the evidence<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;f)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;communicating with others about the advantages and disadvantages of treatments<br class=br> <br class=br><b>4.</b>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<b>Reflect on peoples competences and dispositions by:</b> <br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;a)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;monitoring how they decide which treatment claims to believe and what to do<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;b)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;monitoring how people adjust the processes they use to decide what to believe and do to fit the relevance, importance,<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;and nature of different types of treatment claims and choices<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;c)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;being aware of when people are making treatment claims themselves</td></tr></tbody></table><div class=table-wrap-foot><div class=footnote><a name=d3365e829 class=n-a></a><p id=TFN50> *A good decision is one that makes effective use of the information available to the decision maker at the time the decision is made. A good outcome is one that the decision maker likes. The aim of thinking critically about treatments is to increase the probability of good outcomes (and true conclusions), but many other factors affect outcomes aside from critical thinking<sup><a href="#ref-36">36</a></sup>.</p></div></div></div><a name=T3 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 3. IHC dispositions.</h3></div><table xmlns:ali="http://www.niso.org/schemas/ali/1.0/" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" class=article-table frame=below><a name=d3365e852 class=n-a></a><tbody><a name=d3365e854 class=n-a></a><tr><a name=d3365e856 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e858 class=n-a></a>People should be in the habit of thinking critically about:<br class=br> <br class=br><b>1.</b>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<b>Claims by:</b> <br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;a)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;being aware of treatment claims (including those you make yourself) and choices<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;b)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;questioning the basis for treatment claims<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;c)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;being aware of cognitive biases and going from fast to slow thinking before forming an opinion about a treatment claim,<br class=br> &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;making a claim, or taking a decision<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;d)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;seeking evidence to reduce uncertainty when considering a relevant and important treatment claim or decision<br class=br> <br class=br><b>2.</b>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<b>Evidence used to support claims by:</b> <br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;a)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;questioning the trustworthiness of evidence used to support treatment claims<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;b)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;being alert to misleading presentations of treatment effects<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;c)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;acknowledging and accepting uncertainty about the effects of treatments<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;d)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;being willing to admit errors and modify their judgements when warranted by evidence or a lack of evidence<br class=br> <br class=br><b>3.</b>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<b>Choices by:</b> <br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;a)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;clarifying and understanding the problem, options, and goals when making decisions about treatments<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;b)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;preferring evidence-based sources of information about treatment effects<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;c)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;considering the relevance of the evidence used to inform decisions about treatments<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;d)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;considering effect estimates, baseline risk, the importance of each advantage and disadvantage, the costs, and the<br class=br> &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;certainty of the evidence when making decisions about treatments<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;e)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;making informed judgements about the certainty of estimates of treatment effects<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;f)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;making well-informed decisions<br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;g)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Being aware of how people decide which treatment claims to believe and what to do<br class=br> <br class=br><b>4.</b>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<b>Peoples own thinking by:</b> <br class=br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;a)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Being aware of how people decide which treatment claims to believe and what to do</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class=section><a name=d3365e943 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Other frameworks relevant to the IHC Key Concepts Framework</h3><p class="" id=d3365e948>There are many other frameworks that include concepts, competences, or dispositions that are relevant to thinking critically about treatment claims, comparisons, and choices. These include critical thinking frameworks, logical fallacies and argumentation frameworks, cognitive frameworks, frameworks for scientific thinking, and frameworks related to evidence-based health care. For each category of frameworks there are disagreements about definitions and what is included. For example, learning to think critically is widely held as an aim of education<sup><a href="#ref-9">9</a></sup>, but there is not agreement on the definition of critical thinking and there are several different frameworks (conceptual structures intended to serve as a support or guide) for critical thinking<sup><a href="#ref-10">10</a><a href="#ref-14">14</a></sup>. Similarly, there are different definitions and frameworks for scientific thinking (reasoning and literacy)<sup><a href="#ref-15">15</a><a href="#ref-18">18</a></sup>, epistemic cognition and meta-cognition<sup><a href="#ref-19">19</a>,<a href="#ref-20">20</a></sup>, health literacy<sup><a href="#ref-21">21</a><a href="#ref-23">23</a></sup>, and various aspects of evidence-based health care<sup><a href="#ref-24">24</a><a href="#ref-26">26</a></sup>. There is also overlap across these different framework categories, some of which have been grouped together as frameworks for productive thinking<sup><a href="#ref-12">12</a></sup>.</p></div><div class=section><a name=d3365e996 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Terminology</h3><p class="" id=d3365e1001>Definitions of terms that we use in this paper are shown in <a href="#T4">Table 4</a>.</p><a name=T4 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 4. Definitions of terms as used in this paper.</h3></div><table xmlns:ali="http://www.niso.org/schemas/ali/1.0/" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" class=article-table frame=below><a name=d3365e1018 class=n-a></a><tbody><a name=d3365e1020 class=n-a></a><tr><a name=d3365e1022 class=n-a></a><td to do something) with the intention of achieving a goal, such as<br class=br>improving or maintaining health</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e1033 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 what will happen if one action is<br class=br>chosen compared to what would happen if another action was chosen</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e1055 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 something</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e1064 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1066 class=n-a></a>Concept</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1069 class=n-a></a>In this review, concept (an idea, object of thought, or constituent of thought) refers to a specific type of<br class=br>concept: a criterion (standard for judgment) or principle (a concept that is a guide) for evaluating the<br class=br>trustworthiness of claims and comparisons, and for making choices; or an issue worthy of attention or<br class=br>consideration when assessing claims and making choices.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e1080 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1082 class=n-a></a>Curriculum</td><td align=left voluntary habits of thinking and doing</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e1098 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1100 class=n-a></a>Domain</td><td align=left colspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1121 class=n-a></a>Studies comparing two or more treatments, which are designed, conducted, reported and interpreted<br class=br>to minimize systematic errors (bias) and random errors (resulting from the play of chance) in measuring<br class=br>treatment effects</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e1129 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a doing something or learning to do<br class=br>something, such as thinking critically or learning to think critically about claims, comparisons, and choices</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e1141 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1143 class=n-a></a>Intervention</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1146 class=n-a></a>Any action intended to achieve a goal</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e1150 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1152 class=n-a></a>Skill</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1172 class=n-a></a>Treatment</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1175 class=n-a></a>Any action intended to improve or maintain the health of individuals or communities</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div><div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d3365e1186>Objective</h2><p class="" id=d3365e1189>The objective of our review was to systematically compare the IHC Key Concepts Framework to other frameworks that are relevant to teaching and learning how to think critically about treatment claims, evidence, and choices. We examined similarities and differences between the IHC Key Concepts Framework and other frameworks - particularly in the context of primary and secondary school education - including:</p><div class=list><a name=d3365e1192 class=n-a></a><ul><li><p id=d3365e1196>The purposes and definitions of key terms</p></li><li><p id=d3365e1202>The elements included and domains in which they are grouped</p></li><li><p id=d3365e1208>How the frameworks have been developed and evaluated</p></li><li><p id=d3365e1214>How the frameworks have been used to develop curricula, teaching and learning resources, and assessment tools</p></li></ul></div></div><div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d3365e1222>Methods</h2><p class="" id=d3365e1225>We conducted a systematic review of frameworks relevant to teaching and learning to think critically about treatment claims, evidence used to support those claims, and choices. The protocol for the review is published on our website<sup><a href="#ref-27">27</a></sup>.</p><div class=section><a name=d3365e1232 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Criteria for considering frameworks for inclusion</h3><p class="" id=d3365e1237>We included frameworks that are intended to provide a structure for teaching or learning to think critically about at least one of the following:</p><div class=list><a name=d3365e1240 class=n-a></a><ul><li><p id=d3365e1244>The basis (justification) for claims or arguments about the effects of interventions and the reliability of those justifications</p></li><li><p id=d3365e1250>The extent to which evidence used to support claims about the effects of interventions (comparisons) is fair and reliable</p></li><li><p id=d3365e1256>Choices about what to do in order to achieve a goal</p></li></ul></div><p class="" id=d3365e1261>To be included, the sources for each framework had to include:</p><div class=list><a name=d3365e1264 class=n-a></a><ul><li><p id=d3365e1268>a description of the purpose of the framework;</p></li><li><p id=d3365e1274>a list of the frameworks elements; and</p></li><li><p id=d3365e1280>definitions of the key terms used to describe the purpose of the framework, its elements and domains (in which elements are grouped, if there are any).</p></li></ul></div><p class="" id=d3365e1286>Frameworks that are modifications of another framework were considered together with the framework that had been modified.</p></div><div class=section><a name=d3365e1290 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Search methods for identification of frameworks</h3><p class="" id=d3365e1295>We began by considering 41 frameworks reviewed in <i>Frameworks for Thinking: A Handbook for Teaching and Learning</i><sup><a href="#ref-12">12</a></sup> and frameworks with which we were already familiar<sup><a href="#ref-21">21</a><a href="#ref-35">35</a></sup>. We searched for other relevant frameworks using Google Scholar between October 2018 and June 2019 using the search strategies found in <i>Extended data File 1</i>. We supplemented these searches by conducting citation searches and contacting key informants for each category of the frameworks.</p></div><div class=section><a name=d3365e1315 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Selection of frameworks</h3><p class="" id=d3365e1320>One review author (ADO) initially screened frameworks for possible inclusion. Both review authors then independently assessed full-text articles for each potentially relevant framework using an eligibility form (<i>Extended data File 2</i>). We discussed disagreements and reached a consensus. Frameworks that were assessed for inclusion by both authors and then excluded are listed with the reasons for exclusion in <a href="#T5">Table 5</a>.</p><a name=T5 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 5. Excluded frameworks.</h3></div><table xmlns:ali="http://www.niso.org/schemas/ali/1.0/" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" class=article-table frame=below><a name=d3365e1340 class=n-a></a><thead><a name=d3365e1342 class=n-a></a><tr><a name=d3365e1344 class=n-a></a><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1346 class=n-a></a>Framework</th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1349 class=n-a></a>Reason for exclusion</th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1352 class=n-a></a>Notes</th></tr></thead><tbody><a name=d3365e1357 class=n-a></a><tr><a name=d3365e1359 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1361 class=n-a></a>Bloom taxonomy<sup><a href="#ref-12">12</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1367 class=n-a></a>Does not provide a framework for<br class=br>thinking critically about claims,<br class=br>comparisons or choices</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1374 class=n-a></a>This framework is a way of classifying educational goals in terms<br class=br>of complexity. The initial aim was promoting the exchange of test<br class=br>materials and ideas about testing and of stimulating research on<br class=br>examining and on the relations between examining and education<br class=br>(<a href="#ref-12">12</a>, p. 49). Blooms taxonomy consists of six levels and has a<br class=br>varying amount of detail in the form of sub-categories for each<br class=br>level. The IHC Key Concepts fit into the top level in the original<br class=br>framework - evaluation.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e1396 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1398 class=n-a></a>Altshullers TRIZ Theory of<br class=br>Inventive Problem Solving<sup><a href="#ref-12">12</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1406 class=n-a></a>Does not provide a framework for<br class=br>thinking critically about claims,<br class=br>comparisons or choices</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1413 class=n-a></a>TRIZ is a systematic, creativity and innovation process devised as<br class=br>an aid to practical problem-solving, especially in engineering.<br class=br><br class=br>(<a href="#ref-12">12</a>, p. 122).</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e1425 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1427 class=n-a></a>De Bonos lateral and parallel<br class=br>thinking tools<sup><a href="#ref-12">12</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1435 class=n-a></a>Does not provide a framework for<br class=br>thinking critically about claims,<br class=br>comparisons or choices</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1442 class=n-a></a>The emphasis of this framework is on problem-solving techniques<br class=br>which promote generative, or productive thinking (<a href="#ref-12">12</a>, p. 133).</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e1451 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1453 class=n-a></a>Jewells reasoning taxonomy for<br class=br>gifted children<sup><a href="#ref-12">12</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1461 class=n-a></a>Does not provide a framework for<br class=br>thinking critically about claims,<br class=br>comparisons or choices</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e1468 class=n-a></a>This taxonomy is presented, largely from a philosophical<br class=br>perspective, in response to a perceived need to understand how<br class=br>gifted students think and model of the well-structured problem-solving process.</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class=section><a name=d3365e1736 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Data collection and assessment of included frameworks</h3><p class="" id=d3365e1741>For each included framework, we compiled a list of publications that describe the framework, its development and evaluation, and its use as the basis for curricula, learning resources, and assessment tools.</p><p class="" id=d3365e1744>We recorded independently the following information for each framework, using a data collection form (<i>Extended data File 3</i>):</p><div class=list><a name=d3365e1750 class=n-a></a><ul><li><p id=d3365e1754>Its purpose</p></li><li><p id=d3365e1760>Its domains and elements</p></li><li><p id=d3365e1766>Definitions of key terms used to describe its purpose, domains, or elements</p></li><li><p id=d3365e1772>Methods used to develop the framework</p></li><li><p id=d3365e1778>Methods used to evaluate the framework (if any), and findings</p></li><li><p id=d3365e1785>Ways in which the framework has been used as the basis for</p><div class=list><a name=d3365e1788 class=n-a></a><ul><li><p id=d3365e1792>Curricula</p></li><li><p id=d3365e1798>Teaching and learning</p></li><li><p id=d3365e1804>Assessment tools</p></li></ul></div></li></ul></div><p class="" id=d3365e1811>We compared the data that each of us had collected, discussed disagreements, and reached a consensus.</p><p class="" id=d3365e1815>Based on this information, we assessed independently:</p><div class=list><a name=d3365e1818 class=n-a></a><ul><li><p id=d3365e1822>strengths and weaknesses of how each framework had been developed and evaluated</p></li><li><p id=d3365e1828>strengths and weaknesses of how each framework has been or could be used</p></li><li><p id=d3365e1834>any other strengths or weaknesses</p></li></ul></div><p class="" id=d3365e1839>We compared our assessments, discussed disagreements, and reached a consensus.</p></div><div class=section><a name=d3365e1843 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Analysis of the data</h3><div class=list><a name=d3365e1848 class=n-a></a><ul style="list-style-type: none"><li><p id=d3365e1855><span class=label>1. </span> We summarised key characteristics of the included frameworks in tables.</p></li><li><p id=d3365e1864><span class=label>2. </span> Using Venn diagrams, we mapped the extent to which the purposes of the different frameworks overlap with those of the IHC Key Concepts Framework.</p></li><li><p id=d3365e1873><span class=label>3. </span> We compared the concepts, competences and dispositions in each framework with those in the IHC Key Concepts Framework. We considered separately any elements that could not be categorised as concepts, competences or dispositions.</p></li><li><p id=d3365e1882><span class=label>4. </span> We reflected on our assessments of the frameworks and identified implications for how we might improve the IHC Key Concepts Framework, and its usefulness.</p></li></ul></div><p class="" id=d3365e1887>We conducted these analyses independently and then compared our analyses, discussed disagreements, and reached consensus.</p></div></div><div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d3365e1894>Results</h2><p class="" id=d3365e1897>We screened over 1600 references retrieved using Google Scholar (search strategy: <i>Extended data File 1</i>). In addition, we screened the reference lists in the articles that we retrieved. We identified over 80 frameworks and assessed 35 of these for eligibility based on one or more full-text articles (<a href="#f1">Figure 1</a>). We excluded 13 of these (<a href="#T5">Table 5</a>), so ended up including 22 frameworks (<a href="#T6">Table 6</a>).</p><a name=f1 class=n-a></a><div class="fig panel clearfix"><a target=_blank href="https://f1000researchdata.s3.amazonaws.com/manuscripts/24099/830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure1.gif"><img alt="830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure1.gif" src="https://f1000researchdata.s3.amazonaws.com/manuscripts/24099/830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure1.gif"></a><div class=caption><h3>Figure 1. Flow diagram.</h3><p id=d3365e1922>Frameworks that we grouped together (e.g. health literacy frameworks) are counted as single frameworks.  Frameworks for Thinking: A Handbook for Teaching and Learning (Mosely 2005) has 41 frameworks.  Our primary Google Scholar searches yielded 1588 records.  These frameworks were excluded after being scanned by one of the review authors (ADO).</p></div></div><a name=T6 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 6.
