Instead, they simplified<br class=br>Toulmins framework and then adapted<br class=br>it to incorporate what is currently known<br class=br>about the process of scientific inquiry.<br class=br>They synthesized Toulmin's and Duschl's<br class=br>frameworks to create a framework of<br class=br>scientific reasoning as a distinct mode<br class=br>of thought and discourse with roots in<br class=br>both general argumentation and scientific<br class=br>inquiry.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e2382 class=n-a></a><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2384 class=n-a></a>Cognition</th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e2387 class=n-a></a></th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e2389 class=n-a></a></th></tr><tr><a name=d3365e2392 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2394 class=n-a></a><b>Cognitive biases</b><br class=br><br class=br>Amos Tversky, cognitive and mathematical<br class=br>psychologist and Daniel Kahneman,<br class=br>psychologist and economist, Israel and USA;<br class=br>and others<br class=br><br class=br>1970's<sup><a href="#ref-36">36</a>,<a href="#ref-88">88</a><a href="#ref-96">96</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2420 class=n-a></a>To study and document biases of intuitive<br class=br>thinking in various tasks or beliefs<br class=br>concerning uncertain events. People rely<br class=br>on a limited number of heuristic principles<br class=br>which reduce the complex tasks of<br class=br>assessing probabilities and predicting<br class=br>values to simpler judgmental operations.<br class=br>In general, these heuristics are quite<br class=br>useful, but sometimes they lead to severe<br class=br>and systematic errors.</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2442 class=n-a></a>Tversky and Kahneman are recognised<br class=br>as the founders of cognitive bias theory<br class=br>and their 1974 <i>Science</i> paper was the<br class=br>first codification of the area. They based<br class=br>their classification on their own theory of<br class=br>general judgemental heuristics. The basis<br class=br>for different classifications varies, but they<br class=br>all are based, at least in part, on research<br class=br>evidence of the existence of the included<br class=br>biases.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e2468 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2470 class=n-a></a><b>Framework for understanding people's</b><br class=br><b>theories about their own cognition</b><br class=br><br class=br>John Flavell, developmental psychologist<br class=br>specializing in children's cognitive<br class=br>development, USA; Gregory Schraw and<br class=br>David Moshman, Department of Educational<br class=br>Psychology, University of Nebraska, USA; and<br class=br>others<br class=br><br class=br>1970's<sup><a href="#ref-97">97</a><a href="#ref-107">107</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2500 class=n-a></a>To consider how individuals consolidate<br class=br>different kinds of metacognitive knowledge<br class=br>and regulatory skills into systematized<br class=br>cognitive frameworks, the origin and<br class=br>development of those, and implications for<br class=br>educational research and practice.</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2513 class=n-a></a>Schraw and Moshman<sup><a href="#ref-103">103</a></sup> reviewed<br class=br>standard accounts of metacognition<br class=br>and how metacognitive knowledge and<br class=br>regulation affect cognitive performance.<br class=br>Metacognition, which has been defined in<br class=br>different ways, refers to both knowledge<br class=br>of cognition (an awareness of variables<br class=br>that influence thinking) and regulation<br class=br>of cognition (the ability to regulate one's<br class=br>learning). it is sometimes defined as<br class=br>thinking about thinking.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e2543 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2545 class=n-a></a><b>Epistemological models</b><br class=br><br class=br>Jean Piaget, development psychologist,<br class=br>Switzerland; William Perry Jr., educational<br class=br>psychologist, Harvard, USA; and others<br class=br><br class=br>1950's<sup><a href="#ref-42">42</a><a href="#ref-48">48</a>,<a href="#ref-108">108</a><a href="#ref-113">113</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2572 class=n-a></a>To describe changes in assumptions about<br class=br>sources and certainty of knowledge (the<br class=br>development of epistemic assumptions)<br class=br>and how decisions are justified in light<br class=br>of those assumptions (how epistemic<br class=br>assumptions affect the way individuals<br class=br>understand and solve problems).</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2588 class=n-a></a>Epistemology is an area of philosophy<br class=br>concerned with the nature and justification<br class=br>of human knowledge. A growing area of<br class=br>interest for psychologists and educators<br class=br>is that of personal epistemological<br class=br>development and epistemological beliefs:<br class=br>how individuals come to know, the theories<br class=br>and beliefs they hold about knowing, and<br class=br>the manner in which such epistemological<br class=br>premises are a part of and an influence<br class=br>on the cognitive processes of thinking and<br class=br>reasoning.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e2615 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2617 class=n-a></a><b>AIR model of epistemic cognition</b><br class=br><br class=br>Ravit Duncan, Clark Chinn, Luke Buckland,<br class=br>Graduate School of Education, Rutgers<br class=br>University, USA; Sarit Barzilai, Faculty<br class=br>of Education, University of Haifa, Israel;<br class=br>Ronald Rinehart, Department of educational<br class=br>Psychology and Foundations, University of<br class=br>Northern Iowa, USA<br class=br><br class=br>2014<sup><a href="#ref-114">114</a><a href="#ref-117">117</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2646 class=n-a></a>To help account for how people evaluate<br class=br>information, including inaccurate<br class=br>information and the role that cognitions<br class=br>play in peoples evaluation of inaccurate<br class=br>(as well as accurate) information.</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2657 class=n-a></a>Educational and developmental<br class=br>psychologists have investigated human<br class=br>cognitions about epistemic matters.<br class=br>These are cognitions about a network of<br class=br>interrelated topics including knowledge, its<br class=br>sources and justification, belief, evidence,<br class=br>truth, understanding, explanation, and<br class=br>many others. Different researchers have<br class=br>used different terms for these cognitions,<br class=br>including personal epistemology,<br class=br>epistemological beliefs, epistemic beliefs,<br class=br>epistemic positions, epistemic cognition,<br class=br>epistemological reflection, and reflective<br class=br>judgment.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e2689 class=n-a></a><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2691 class=n-a></a>Scientific thinking</th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e2694 class=n-a></a></th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e2696 class=n-a></a></th></tr><tr><a name=d3365e2699 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2701 class=n-a></a><b>PISA framework for scientific literacy</b><br class=br><br class=br>Organisation for Economic Co-operation<br class=br>and Development (OECD). The Programme<br class=br>for International Student Assessment (PISA)<br class=br>is a collaborative effort among the OECD<br class=br>member governments to provide a new kind<br class=br>of assessment of student achievement on a<br class=br>recurring basis.<br class=br><br class=br>1997<sup><a href="#ref-118">118</a><a href="#ref-124">124</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2730 class=n-a></a>The main benefit of constructing and<br class=br>validating the framework is improved<br class=br>measurement. Other potential benefits<br class=br>include: a common language, an analysis<br class=br>of the kinds of knowledge and skills<br class=br>associated with successful performance,<br class=br>and identifying and understanding<br class=br>particular variables that underlie<br class=br>successful performance.</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2750 class=n-a></a>PISA is designed to collect information<br class=br>through three-yearly cycles and presents<br class=br>data on the reading, mathematical and<br class=br>scientific literacy of 15-year-old students,<br class=br>schools and countries. It provides<br class=br>insights into the factors that influence<br class=br>the development of skills and attitudes<br class=br>at home and at school, and examines<br class=br>how these factors interact and what the<br class=br>implications are for policy development.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e2773 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2775 class=n-a></a><b>Framework for K-12 science education</b><br class=br><br class=br>National Research Council (NRC) Committee<br class=br>on a Conceptual Framework for New K-12<br class=br>Science Education Standards, USA. The<br class=br>committee included professionals in the natural<br class=br>sciences, mathematics, engineering, cognitive<br class=br>and developmental psychology, the learning<br class=br>sciences, education policy and implementation,<br class=br>research on learning science in the classroom,<br class=br>and the practice of teaching science.<br class=br><br class=br>2010<sup><a href="#ref-125">125</a><a href="#ref-132">132</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2809 class=n-a></a>To articulate a broad set of expectations<br class=br>for students in science. The overarching<br class=br>goal is to ensure that by the end of<br class=br>12th grade, all students have some<br class=br>appreciation of the beauty and wonder<br class=br>of science; possess sufficient knowledge<br class=br>of science and engineering to engage<br class=br>in public discussions on related issues;<br class=br>are careful consumers of scientific and<br class=br>technological information related to their<br class=br>everyday lives; are able to continue to<br class=br>learn about science outside school;<br class=br>and have the skills to enter careers of<br class=br>their choice, including (but not limited<br class=br>to) careers in science, engineering, and<br class=br>technology.</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2844 class=n-a></a>The framework was the first part of a<br class=br>two-stage process to produce a next<br class=br>generation set of science standards for<br class=br>voluntary adoption by states in the USA.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e2854 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2856 class=n-a></a><b>Systems thinking</b><br class=br><br class=br>Ideas about holistic thinking and change<br class=br>processes can be traced back to the ancient<br class=br>Greeks. The start of modern systems thinking<br class=br>is attributed the articulation of systems ideas<br class=br>by Ludwig von Bertalanffy, an Austrian biologist<br class=br>who started lecturing and writing in the 1930's<br class=br>on what he called general system theory; and<br class=br>to Aleksandr Bogdanov, a Russian revolutionary,<br class=br>philosopher and scientist.<br class=br><br class=br>1910's<sup><a href="#ref-133">133</a><a href="#ref-142">142</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2890 class=n-a></a>To understand and interpret complex<br class=br>systems in order to navigate information,<br class=br>make decisions, and solve problems.</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2897 class=n-a></a>Systems theory is the transdisciplinary<br class=br>study of the abstract organisation<br class=br>of phenomena, independent of their<br class=br>substance, type, or spatial and<br class=br>temporal scale. Systems can be used<br class=br>to represent the complex organisation<br class=br>of virtually any collection of real-world<br class=br>entities into an ordered form that we can<br class=br>better understand. There are several<br class=br>conceptualizations of systems thinking in<br class=br>education.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e2923 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2925 class=n-a></a><b>Model for scientific thinking</b><br class=br><br class=br>Gregory Feist, Department of Psychology,<br class=br>College of William &amp; Mary, USA; Carlo Magno,<br class=br>Counselling and Educational Psychology, De La<br class=br>Salle University, Philippines<br class=br><br class=br>1990's<sup><a href="#ref-143">143</a>,<a href="#ref-144">144</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2948 class=n-a></a>To investigate the relationship of the<br class=br>constructs scientific thinking, self-<br class=br>regulation in research, and creativity in a<br class=br>measurement model.</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2957 class=n-a></a>Feist investigated whether personality traits<br class=br>consistently distinguish artists from non-<br class=br>artists and scientists from non-scientists.<br class=br>Magno<sup><a href="#ref-144">144</a></sup>, building on Feist's work<sup><a href="#ref-143">143</a></sup>,<br class=br>investigated the relationship between<br class=br>scientific thinking, self-regulation, and<br class=br>creativity.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e2982 class=n-a></a><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2984 class=n-a></a>Evidence-based health care</th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e2987 class=n-a></a></th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e2989 class=n-a></a></th></tr><tr><a name=d3365e2992 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e2994 class=n-a></a><b>Health literacy frameworks</b><br class=br><br class=br>The term health literacy was first coined in<br class=br>1974 by Scott Simonds, Professor of Health<br class=br>Education, University of Michigan, School of<br class=br>Public Health, USA. Several frameworks have<br class=br>been developed since then.<br class=br><br class=br>1970's<sup><a href="#ref-21">21</a><a href="#ref-23">23</a>,<a href="#ref-145">145</a><a href="#ref-149">149</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3025 class=n-a></a>To develop health literacy enhancing<br class=br>interventions and to develop and validate<br class=br>of measurement tools.</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3032 class=n-a></a>Simonds wrote in 1974 that: Minimum<br class=br>standards for 'health literacy' should be<br class=br>established for all grade levels K through<br class=br>12. Those school districts that fall below<br class=br>standard should be provided with federal<br class=br>aid to develop programs with teachers<br class=br>qualified to teach health education"<sup><a href="#ref-150">150</a>,<a href="#ref-151">151</a></sup>.<br class=br>Since then, it has been estimated that<br class=br>approximately 80 million Americans have<br class=br>limited health literacy, and multiple studies<br class=br>have found that low health literacy is<br class=br>associated with poorer health outcomes<br class=br>and poorer use of health care services<sup><a href="#ref-152">152</a></sup>.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3073 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3075 class=n-a></a><b>Evidence-based practice (EBP) core</b><br class=br><b>competencies</b><br class=br><br class=br>International EBP leaders led by team at Bond<br class=br>University, Australia<br class=br><br class=br>2018<sup><a href="#ref-26">26</a>,<a href="#ref-153">153</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3097 class=n-a></a>To develop a consensus-based set of core<br class=br>EBP competencies that EBP teaching and<br class=br>learning programs should cover</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3104 class=n-a></a>The term evidence-based medicine was<br class=br>first developed in the field of medicine in<br class=br>the early 1990s, but as its use expanded to<br class=br>include other health disciplines, it became<br class=br>known as EBP. EBP provides a framework<br class=br>for the integration of research evidence<br class=br>and patients values and preferences into<br class=br>the delivery of health care. Although many<br class=br>teaching strategies have been used and<br class=br>evaluated, a lack of EBP knowledge and<br class=br>skills is still one of the most commonly<br class=br>reported barriers to practicing EBP.<br class=br>One of the potential explanations is the<br class=br>inconsistency in the quality and content of<br class=br>the EBP teaching programs.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3138 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3140 class=n-a></a><b>GRADE (and related frameworks)</b><br class=br><br class=br>The GRADE Working Group, which includes<br class=br>methodologists, health researchers, systematic<br class=br>review authors, guideline developers<br class=br><br class=br>2000<sup><a href="#ref-25">25</a>,<a href="#ref-30">30</a>,<a href="#ref-154">154</a><a href="#ref-160">160</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3167 class=n-a></a>Grading of Recommendations<br class=br>Assessment, Development, and<br class=br>Evaluation (GRADE) offers a transparent<br class=br>and structured process for developing<br class=br>and presenting summaries of evidence,<br class=br>including its quality, for systematic reviews<br class=br>and recommendations in health care. The<br class=br>purpose of Evidence to Decision (EtD)<br class=br>frameworks is to help people use evidence<br class=br>in a structured and transparent way to<br class=br>inform decisions in the context of clinical<br class=br>recommendations, coverage decisions,<br class=br>and health system or public health<br class=br>recommendations and decisions.</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3198 class=n-a></a>Since the 1970s a growing number<br class=br>of organisations have employed<br class=br>various systems to grade the quality<br class=br>(level) of evidence and the strength of<br class=br>recommendations. Different organisations<br class=br>have used different systems, resulting<br class=br>in confusion and impeding effective<br class=br>communication. The GRADE Working<br class=br>Group began as an informal collaboration<br class=br>of people with an interest in tackling the<br class=br>shortcomings of prior grading systems.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3223 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3225 class=n-a></a><b>Bradford-Hill criteria</b><br class=br><br class=br>Austin Bradford Hill, Professor Emeritus of<br class=br>Medical Statistics, University of London, UK<br class=br><br class=br>1965<sup><a href="#ref-28">28</a>,<a href="#ref-161">161</a><a href="#ref-165">165</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3246 class=n-a></a>To address: "What aspects of an<br class=br>association between two variables should<br class=br>we especially consider before deciding<br class=br>that the most likely interpretation of it is<br class=br>causation?"</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3257 class=n-a></a>This framework was developed to identify<br class=br>the causes of diseases and particularly<br class=br>to determine the role of smoking in lung<br class=br>cancer, but its use has been extended to<br class=br>public health decision making, a domain<br class=br>where questions about causal effects<br class=br>relate to the consequences of interventions<br class=br>that have often been motivated by the<br class=br>identification of causal factors. It has<br class=br>proven useful and has driven decision<br class=br>making in public health for decades.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3283 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3285 class=n-a></a><b>Critical appraisal</b><br class=br><br class=br>International teachers of evidence-based health<br class=br>care and research methodologists<br class=br><br class=br>1981<sup><a href="#ref-29">29</a>,<a href="#ref-166">166</a><a href="#ref-179">179</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3306 class=n-a></a>To teach critical appraisal of health<br class=br>research. However, some critical appraisal<br class=br>tools are intended primarily for critically<br class=br>appraising research in the context of<br class=br>systematic reviews and some are intended<br class=br>primarily for reporting standards. There is<br class=br>an overlap among these tools and clear<br class=br>distinctions are sometimes not made<br class=br>among tools with different purposes.</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3326 class=n-a></a>The strategies we shall suggest assume<br class=br>that clinical readers are already behind in<br class=br>their reading and that they will never have<br class=br>more time to read than they do now. For<br class=br>this reason, and because the guides that<br class=br>follow call for closer attention to "Materials<br class=br>and methods" and other matters that often<br class=br>appear in small type, many of the guides<br class=br>recommend tossing an article aside as not<br class=br>worth reading, usually on the basis of quite<br class=br>preliminary evidence. It is only through<br class=br>the early rejection of most articles that<br class=br>busy clinicians can focus on the few that<br class=br>are both valid and applicable in their own<br class=br>practices.<sup><a href="#ref-170">170</a></sup></td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3363 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3365 class=n-a></a><b>Cochrane risk of bias tool (and related</b><br class=br><b>frameworks)</b><br class=br><br class=br>International health research methodologists<br class=br><br class=br>1980's<sup><a href="#ref-29">29</a>,<a href="#ref-31">31</a>,<a href="#ref-33">33</a>,<a href="#ref-180">180</a><a href="#ref-184">184</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3393 class=n-a></a>To assess the risk of bias in randomised<br class=br>and non-randomised studies (sometimes<br class=br>referred to as quality or internal validity).<br class=br>Assessments of risk of bias are intended<br class=br>to help interpret findings and explain<br class=br>heterogeneity in systematic reviews;<br class=br>in addition, reviews use risk-of-bias<br class=br>assessments of individual studies in<br class=br>grading the certainty of the evidence.<br class=br>Reviews may exclude studies assessed as<br class=br>high risk of bias.</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3417 class=n-a></a>"The concern about study quality<br class=br>first arose in the early 1980s with the<br class=br>publication of a landmark paper by Tom<br class=br>Chalmers and colleagues and another<br class=br>extensive work by Hemminki, who<br class=br>evaluated the quality of trials done in 1965<br class=br>through 1975 that were used to support<br class=br>the licensing of drugs in Finland and<br class=br>Sweden<sup><a href="#ref-185">185</a></sup>.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3442 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3444 class=n-a></a><b>Catalogue of biases</b><br class=br><br class=br>Centre for Evidence Based Medicine, Oxford<br class=br>University, UK<br class=br><br class=br>2017<sup><a href="#ref-34">34</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3459 class=n-a></a>To obtain the least biased information,<br class=br>researchers must acknowledge the<br class=br>potential presence of biases and take<br class=br>steps to avoid and minimise their effects.<br class=br>Equally, in assessing the results of studies,<br class=br>we must be aware of the different types of<br class=br>biases, their potential impact and how this<br class=br>affects interpretation and use of evidence<br class=br>in health care decision making. To better<br class=br>understand the persistent presence,<br class=br>diversity, and impact of biases, we are<br class=br>compiling a Catalogue of Biases, stemming<br class=br>from original work by David Sackett. The<br class=br>entries are a work in progress and describe<br class=br>a wide range of biases  outlining their<br class=br>potential impact in research studies.</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3494 class=n-a></a>David Sackett, in his 1979 paper Bias in<br class=br>Analytic Research<sup><a href="#ref-186">186</a></sup>, reported the first<br class=br>draft of a catalog of biases which may<br class=br>distort the design, execution, analysis,<br class=br>and interpretation of research. Sackett<br class=br>catalogued 35 biases that arise in the<br class=br>context of clinical trials and listed 56<br class=br>biases potentially affecting case-control<br class=br>and cohort studies. He proposed the<br class=br>continued development of an annotated<br class=br>catalogue of bias as a priority for research.<br class=br>He suggested that each citation should<br class=br>include a useful definition, a referenced<br class=br>example illustrating the magnitude and<br class=br>direction of its effects, and a description of<br class=br>the appropriate preventive measures if any.</td></tr></tbody></table></div><p class="" id=d3365e3537>We included four frameworks on critical thinking, three on logic and argumentation, four on cognition, four on scientific thinking, and seven on evidence-based healthcare. We grouped several frameworks together for five types of frameworks - logical fallacies, cognitive biases, epistemological models, systems thinking, and health literacy. We also considered related frameworks together with the <i>Grading of Recommendations Assessment, Development, and Evaluation (GRADE) framework</i> and the <i>Cochrane Risk of Bias Tool</i>. The purpose and background of each of the included frameworks are shown in <a href="#T6">Table 6</a>, and definitions of the key term for each framework are shown in <a href="#T7">Table 7</a>.</p><a name=T7 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 7. Definitions of the core term for each included framework.</h3></div><table xmlns:ali="http://www.niso.org/schemas/ali/1.0/" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" name=d3365e3573 class=n-a></a>Definitions</th></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3577 class=n-a></a><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3579 class=n-a></a>Critical thinking</th><th valign=top><a name=d3365e3590 class=n-a></a>Taxonomy of critical thinking<br class=br>dispositions and abilities</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3595 class=n-a></a>Critical thinking is reasonable reflective thinking focused on deciding what to believe or do.<br class=br><a target=xrefwindow href="http://criticalthinking.net/index.php/longdefinition/" id=d3365e3598>http://criticalthinking.net/index.php/longdefinition/</a></td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3602 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3604 class=n-a></a>Model of critical thinking</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3607 class=n-a></a>Critical thinking is the intellectually disciplined process of actively and skilfully conceptualizing, applying,<br class=br>analysing, synthesizing, and/or evaluating information gathered from, or generated by, observation,<br class=br>experience, reflection, reasoning, or communication, as a guide to belief and action.<br class=br><a target=xrefwindow href="http://www.criticalthinking.org/pages/defining-critical-thinking/766" id=d3365e3614>http://www.criticalthinking.org/pages/defining-critical-thinking/766</a></td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3618 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3620 class=n-a></a>List of critical thinking skills</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3623 class=n-a></a>Critical thinking is the use of those cognitive skills or strategies that increase the probability of a<br class=br>desirable outcome. It is purposeful, reasonable, and goal directed. Also known as directed thinking<sup><a href="#ref-68">68</a></sup>.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3633 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3635 class=n-a></a>Model of the good thinker</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3638 class=n-a></a>The definition of rationality as the kind of thinking that helps us achieve our goals. A good decision is<br class=br>one that makes effective use of the information available to the decision maker at the time the decision<br class=br>is made. A good outcome is one that the decision maker likes. The whole point of good thinking is<br class=br>to increase the probability of good outcomes (and true conclusions), but many other factors affect<br class=br>outcomes aside from good thinking. Good decision making involves sufficient search for possibilities,<br class=br> evidence, and goals, and fairness in the search for evidence and in inference<sup><a href="#ref-36">36</a></sup>.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3657 class=n-a></a><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3659 class=n-a></a>Logic and argumentation</th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e3662 class=n-a></a></th></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3666 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3668 class=n-a></a>Logical fallacies</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3671 class=n-a></a>Fallacy is the use of invalid or otherwise faulty reasoning in the construction of an argument.<br class=br><a target=xrefwindow href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fallacy" id=d3365e3674>https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fallacy</a></td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3678 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3680 class=n-a></a>Taxonomy of concepts and<br class=br>critical abilities related to the<br class=br>evaluation of verbal arguments</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3687 class=n-a></a>The evaluation of verbal arguments is the process of applying higher-order concepts (i.e., rules or<br class=br>principles concerning the nature, structure, and tests of argument) to arguments occurring in ordinary<br class=br>verbal discourse in order to assess their acceptability. Such an evaluation requires that one understand<br class=br>numerous concepts and employ diverse critical abilities<sup><a href="#ref-85">85</a></sup>.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3701 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3703 class=n-a></a>Evidence based reasoning<br class=br>framework</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3708 class=n-a></a>To participate in arguments about scientific ideas, students must learn how to evaluate and use<br class=br>evidence. That is, apart from what they may already know about the substance of an assertion, students<br class=br>who are scientifically literate should be able to make judgments based on the evidence supporting or<br class=br>refuting that assertion<sup><a href="#ref-87">87</a></sup>.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3722 class=n-a></a><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3724 class=n-a></a>Cognition</th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e3727 class=n-a></a></th></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3730 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3732 class=n-a></a>Cognitive biases</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3735 class=n-a></a>Cognitive biases are systematic patterns of deviation from norm or rationality in judgment.<br class=br><a target=xrefwindow href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_cognitive_biases" id=d3365e3738>https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_cognitive_biases</a></td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3742 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3744 class=n-a></a>Framework for understanding<br class=br>people's theories about their<br class=br>own cognition</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3751 class=n-a></a>Metacognitive theories are theories that integrate one's knowledge about cognition and regulation of<br class=br>cognition. By "theory" we mean a relatively systematic structure of knowledge that can be used to<br class=br>explain and predict a broad range of empirical phenomena. By a "metacognitive theory" we mean a<br class=br>relatively systematic structure of knowledge that can be used to explain and predict a broad range of<br class=br>cognitive and metacognitive phenomena<sup><a href="#ref-103">103</a></sup>.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3768 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3770 class=n-a></a>Epistemological models</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3773 class=n-a></a>Definitions of critical thinking are numerous and wide-ranging. However, one non-controversial claim<br class=br>we can make about critical thinking is that it entails awareness of ones own thinking and reflection on<br class=br>the thinking of self and others as an object of cognition. Metacognition, a construct that is assuming an<br class=br>increasingly central place in cognitive development research, is defined in similar terms as awareness<br class=br>and management of ones own thought, or thinking about thinking. Metacognition originates early in<br class=br>life, when children first become aware of their own and others minds. But like many other intellectual<br class=br>skills, metacognitive skills typically do not develop to the level we would like<sup><a href="#ref-47">47</a></sup>.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3794 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3796 class=n-a></a>AIR model of epistemic<br class=br>cognition</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3801 class=n-a></a>Epistemic cognition refers to the complex of cognitions that are related to the achievement of epistemic ends;<br class=br>notable epistemic ends include knowledge, understanding, useful models, explanations, and the like<sup><a href="#ref-116">116</a></sup>.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3811 class=n-a></a><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3813 class=n-a></a>Scientific thinking</th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e3816 class=n-a></a></th></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3819 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3821 class=n-a></a>PISA framework for scientific<br class=br>literacy</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3826 class=n-a></a>Scientific literacy is an individuals scientific knowledge and use of that knowledge to identify questions,<br class=br>to acquire new knowledge, to explain scientific phenomena, and to draw evidence-based conclusions<br class=br>about science-related issues, understanding of the characteristic features of science as a form of human<br class=br>knowledge and enquiry, awareness of how science and technology shape our material, intellectual,<br class=br>and cultural environments, and willingness to engage in science-related issues, and with the ideas of<br class=br>science, as a reflective citizen<sup><a href="#ref-122">122</a></sup>.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3845 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3847 class=n-a></a>Framework for K-12 science<br class=br>education</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3852 class=n-a></a>Science, engineering, and the technologies they influence permeate every aspect of modern life. Indeed,<br class=br>some knowledge of science and engineering is required to engage with the major public policy issues<br class=br>of today as well as to make informed everyday decisions, such as selecting among alternative medical<br class=br>treatments or determining how to invest public funds for water supply options. In addition, understanding<br class=br>science and the extraordinary insights it has produced can be meaningful and relevant on a personal<br class=br>level, opening new worlds to explore and offering lifelong opportunities for enriching peoples lives. In<br class=br>these contexts, learning science is important for everyone, even those who eventually choose careers in<br class=br>fields other than science or engineering. By framework we mean a broad description of the content and<br class=br>sequence of learning expected of all students by the completion of high schoolbut not at the level of<br class=br>detail of grade-by-grade standards or, at the high school level, course descriptions and standards. Instead,<br class=br>as this document lays out, the framework is intended as a guide to standards developers as well as for<br class=br>curriculum designers, assessment developers, state and district science administrators, professionals<br class=br>responsible for science teacher education, and science educators working in informal settings<sup><a href="#ref-32">32</a></sup>.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3886 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3888 class=n-a></a>Systems thinking</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3891 class=n-a></a>System thinking is the ability to understand and interpret complex systems. Our conceptualisation of<br class=br>systems thinking is based on Riess and Mischos definition: as the ability to recognise, describe, model<br class=br>(e.g. to structure, to organise) and to explain complex aspects of reality as systems. According to this<br class=br>definition, Riess and Mischo stressed essential aspects of systems thinking, which include the ability to<br class=br>identify important elements of systems and the varied interdependency between these elements, the<br class=br>ability to recognise dimensions of time dynamics, the ability to construct an internal model of reality and<br class=br>the ability to give explanations, to make prognoses and to develop means and strategies of action based<br class=br>on that model<sup><a href="#ref-141">141</a></sup>.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3915 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3917 class=n-a></a>Model for scientific thinking</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3920 class=n-a></a>Scientific thinking is composed of a set of characteristics that includes practical inclination, analytical<br class=br>interest, intellectual independence, and assertiveness<sup><a href="#ref-144">144</a></sup>. Broadly defined, scientific thinking includes<br class=br>the skills involved in inquiry, experimentation, evidence evaluation, and inference that are done in the<br class=br>service of conceptual change or scientific understanding. Scientific thinking is defined as the application<br class=br>of the methods or principles of scientific inquiry to reasoning or problem-solving situations, and involves<br class=br>the skills implicated in generating, testing and revising theories, and in the case of fully developed<br class=br>skills, to reflect on the process of knowledge acquisition and change. Participants engage in some or all<br class=br>the components of scientific inquiry, such as designing experiments, evaluating evidence and making<br class=br>inferences<sup><a href="#ref-16">16</a></sup>.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3949 class=n-a></a><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3951 class=n-a></a>Evidence-based health care</th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e3954 class=n-a></a></th></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3957 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3959 class=n-a></a>Health literacy frameworks</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3962 class=n-a></a>There are various definitions of health literacy. A new all inclusive comprehensive definition capturing<br class=br>the essence of the 17 definitions identified in the literature is: Health literacy is linked to literacy and<br class=br>entails peoples knowledge, motivation and competences to access, understand, appraise, and apply<br class=br>health information to make judgments and take decisions in everyday life concerning healthcare,<br class=br>disease prevention and health promotion to maintain or improve quality of life during the life course<sup><a href="#ref-22">22</a></sup>.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3978 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3980 class=n-a></a>EBP core competencies</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3983 class=n-a></a>Evidence-Based Practice (EBP) is the integration of the best research evidence with clinical expertise<br class=br>and patients unique values and circumstances. Core competencies are defined as the essential minimal<br class=br>set of a combination of attributes, such as applied knowledge, skills, and attitudes, that enable an<br class=br>individual to perform a set of tasks to an appropriate standard efficiently and effectively<sup><a href="#ref-26">26</a></sup>.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e3997 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e3999 class=n-a></a>GRADE and related<br class=br>frameworks</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e4004 class=n-a></a>Quality of evidence (also referred to as certainty of the evidence or certainty of the anticipated<br class=br>effect) is the extent to which one can be confident that an estimate of effect is correct. Strength<br class=br>of the recommendation is the degree of confidence that the desirable effects of adherence to a<br class=br>recommendation outweigh the undesirable effects.<br class=br><a target=xrefwindow href="https://gdt.gradepro.org/app/handbook/handbook.html#h.svwngs6pm0f2" id=d3365e4013>https://gdt.gradepro.org/app/handbook/handbook.html#h.svwngs6pm0f2</a></td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e4017 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e4019 class=n-a></a>Bradford Hill criteria</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e4022 class=n-a></a>An association (or correlation) in statistics is a relationship between two variables in a study, e.g.<br class=br>between having received a particular treatment and having experienced a particular outcome. Causation<br class=br>(a causal association) is an association between two variables where a change in one makes a change<br class=br>in the other one happen.<br class=br><a target=xrefwindow href="http://getitglossary.org/" id=d3365e4031>http://getitglossary.org/</a></td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e4036 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e4038 class=n-a></a>Critical appraisal</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e4041 class=n-a></a>"Critical appraisal is the systematic evaluation of clinical research papers in order to establish:<br class=br>1. Does this study address a clearly focused question?<br class=br>2. Did the study use valid methods to address this question?<br class=br>3. Are the valid results of this study important?<br class=br>4. Are these valid, important results applicable to my patient or population?"<br class=br><a target=xrefwindow href="https://www.cebm.net/2014/06/critical-appraisal/" id=d3365e4052>https://www.cebm.net/2014/06/critical-appraisal/</a></td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e4056 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e4058 class=n-a></a>Risk of bias</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e4061 class=n-a></a>Bias is the result of flaws in design, conduct, analyses, and reporting, leading to underestimation or<br class=br>overestimation of the true intervention effect. It is usually impossible to know the extent to which biases<br class=br>have affected the results of a particular trial<sup><a href="#ref-31">31</a></sup>.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e4073 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e4075 class=n-a></a>Catalogue of biases</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e4078 class=n-a></a>Biases (systematic errors) distort effect estimates away from actual effects. Biases are caused by<br class=br>inadequacies in the design, conduct, analysis, reporting, or interpretation of treatment comparisons.<br class=br>Because it is generally not possible to know the degree to which an effect estimate is biased,<br class=br>judgements must be made about the risk of bias using criteria that assess factors that are known, or<br class=br>thought to be associated with bias, such as unconcealed allocation of participants to treatments. In<br class=br>everyday language, bias has other meanings, for example prejudice.<br class=br><a target=xrefwindow href="http://getitglossary.org/term/bias" id=d3365e4092>http://getitglossary.org/term/bias</a></td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class=section><a name=d3365e4099 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Comparison of the included frameworks to the IHC Key Concepts Framework</h3><p class="" id=d3365e4104>We summarise our comparison of the included frameworks to the IHC Key Concepts Framework in <a href="#T8">Table 8</a>. Two frameworks had a similar purpose: Ennis <i>taxonomy of critical thinking dispositions and abilities</i><sup><a href="#ref-12">12</a>,<a href="#ref-50">50</a><a href="#ref-59">59</a></sup> and Barons <i>model of the good thinker</i><sup><a href="#ref-12">12</a>,<a href="#ref-36">36</a>,<a href="#ref-69">69</a><a href="#ref-75">75</a></sup>. Ennis goal is for students to learn to think critically about what to believe or do. Barons goal is for students to learn to think more rationally, that is, in a way that helps them to achieve their goals. Both those goals are broader than that of the IHC Key Concepts Framework, which is to enable people to make informed decisions about which claims to believe about the effects of things they can do (interventions) for their health, the health of others or for other reasons, and about what to do to achieve their goals<sup><a href="#ref-4">4</a></sup>. The purposes of the two other critical thinking frameworks that we included (the <i>Model of critical thinking</i> and <i>List of critical thinking skills</i>) were also somewhat like the purpose of the IHC Key Concepts Framework.</p><a name=T8 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 8. style="background-color:#F2F2F2" valign=top><a name=d3365e4250 class=n-a></a>Yes</td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 style="background-color:#F2F2F2" valign=top><a name=d3365e4254 valign=top><a name=d3365e4303 class=n-a></a>Logic and argumentation</th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e4306 class=n-a></a></th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e4308 class=n-a></a></th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e4310 class=n-a></a></th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e4312 class=n-a></a></th><th align=left colspan=1 clearfix"><div class=caption><h3></h3></div><table xmlns:ali="http://www.niso.org/schemas/ali/1.0/" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" class=article-table frame=below><a name=d3365e4769 class=n-a></a><tbody><a name=d3365e4771 class=n-a></a><tr><a name=d3365e4773 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e4775 class=n-a></a>* Similarity to the IHC framework:</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 style="background-color:#808080" valign=top><a name=d3365e4778 class=n-a></a><b>Similar</b></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 style="background-color:#BFBFBF" valign=top><a name=d3365e4782 class=n-a></a><b>Some similarity</b></td><td name=d3365e4790 class=n-a></a><b>Not similar</b></td></tr></tbody></table></div><a name=T14 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3></h3></div><table xmlns:ali="http://www.niso.org/schemas/ali/1.0/" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" class=article-table frame=below><a name=d3365e4800 class=n-a></a><tbody><a name=d3365e4802 class=n-a></a><tr><a name=d3365e4804 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e4806 class=n-a></a>Overlap with the IHC framework:</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 style="background-color:#BFBFBF" valign=top><a name=d3365e4809 class=n-a></a><b>Some overlap</b></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 style="background-color:#F2F2F2" valign=top><a name=d3365e4813 class=n-a></a><b>Little overlap</b></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e4817 class=n-a></a><b>No overlap</b></td></tr></tbody></table><div class=table-wrap-foot><div class=footnote><a name=d3365e4826 class=n-a></a><p id=TFN8>  Yes = included in the framework; No = not included in the framework</p></div></div></div><p class="" id=d3365e4835><a href="#f2">Figure 2</a> illustrates how we view the relationship between critical thinking and the IHC Key Concepts Framework. Although the IHC framework focuses specifically on critical thinking about health effects and choices, the same Key Concepts can be applied to many other types of interventions (actions) and decisions<sup><a href="#ref-7">7</a></sup>. Because achieving our goals depends on what we do (actions), deciding what to believe about the possible effects of our actions and what to do is at the centre of critical thinking. However, critical thinking also applies to many other types of beliefs, such as beliefs about religion, history, or art.</p><a name=f2 class=n-a></a><div class="fig panel clearfix"><a target=_blank href="https://f1000researchdata.s3.amazonaws.com/manuscripts/24099/830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure2.gif"><img alt="830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure2.gif" src="https://f1000researchdata.s3.amazonaws.com/manuscripts/24099/830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure2.gif"></a><div class=caption><h3>Figure 2. Venn diagram showing the relationship between critical thinking and the IHC framework.</h3></div></div><p class="" id=d3365e4858>The goal of the IHC Key Concepts Framework is To enable people to make good decisions about which claims to believe about the effects of things they can do for their health, the health of others or for other reasons, and about what to do to achieve their goals<sup><a href="#ref-4">4</a></sup>. Our formulation of that goal was influenced by how Ennis and Baron formulated their goals. We have adapted Barons definition of a good decision<sup><a href="#ref-36">36</a></sup> to explain what this means: a good decision is one that makes effective use of the information available to the decision maker at the time the decision is made. A good outcome is one that the decision maker likes. The aim of thinking critically about treatments is to increase the probability of good outcomes (and true conclusions), but many other factors affect outcomes aside from critical thinking.</p><p class="" id=d3365e4869>The purpose of one of the logic and argumentation frameworks that we included had a somewhat similar purpose to that of the IHC Key Concepts Framework. The <i>evidence-based reasoning framework</i><sup><a href="#ref-87">87</a></sup> was developed as an analytic tool intended as a foundation for assessing students ability to reason from evidence in writing and classroom discussions. The relationship between argumentation  critical evaluation of arguments  and the IHC Key Concepts Framework is illustrated in <a href="#f3">Figure 3</a>. The purposes of four of the evidence-based health care frameworks were also somewhat similar to the purpose of the IHC Key Concepts Framework: health literacy<sup><a href="#ref-21">21</a><a href="#ref-23">23</a>,<a href="#ref-145">145</a><a href="#ref-149">149</a></sup>, the <i>Evidence-based practice (EBP) core competencies</i><sup><a href="#ref-26">26</a></sup>, <i>GRADE</i><sup><a href="#ref-25">25</a>,<a href="#ref-30">30</a>,<a href="#ref-154">154</a><a href="#ref-160">160</a></sup>, and critical appraisal tools<sup><a href="#ref-29">29</a>,<a href="#ref-166">166</a><a href="#ref-179">179</a></sup>.</p><a name=f3 class=n-a></a><div class="fig panel clearfix"><a target=_blank href="https://f1000researchdata.s3.amazonaws.com/manuscripts/24099/830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure3.gif"><img alt="830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure3.gif" src="https://f1000researchdata.s3.amazonaws.com/manuscripts/24099/830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure3.gif"></a><div class=caption><h3>Figure 3. Venn diagram showing the relationship between argumentation and the IHC framework.</h3></div></div><p class="" id=d3365e4940><a href="#f4">Figure 4</a> illustrates the relationship between the evidence-based practice framework and the IHC Key Concepts Framework. Evidence-based practice is a framework for health professionals, whereas the IHC Key Concepts Framework is for young people, patients and the public, and policymakers, as well as health professionals. Evidence-based practice is a broader framework, which includes critical appraisal of other types of evidence besides evidence of effects. It also includes formulating clinical questions, acquiring evidence, and evaluating performance, which are largely outside of the scope of the IHC Key Concepts Framework. The aim of evidence-based practice is to improve health outcomes, and that depends on what health professionals, patients and the public do. Thus, the IHC Key Concepts Framework  critical thinking about effects and choices  is at the centre of evidence-based practice, in much the same way as it is at the centre of critical thinking.</p><a name=f4 class=n-a></a><div class="fig panel clearfix"><a target=_blank href="https://f1000researchdata.s3.amazonaws.com/manuscripts/24099/830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure4.gif"><img alt="830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure4.gif" src="https://f1000researchdata.s3.amazonaws.com/manuscripts/24099/830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure4.gif"></a><div class=caption><h3>Figure 4. Venn diagram showing the relationship between evidence-based practice and the IHC framework.</h3></div></div><p class="" id=d3365e4960>Health literacy also has a broader focus than the IHC Key Concepts Framework. This is most clearly illustrated by Nutbeams framework<sup><a href="#ref-145">145</a>,<a href="#ref-148">148</a></sup>, which divides health literacy into functional, interactive, and critical health literacy. The IHC Key Concepts Framework is most closely related to critical health literacy, as illustrated in <a href="#f5">Figure 5</a>.</p><a name=f5 class=n-a></a><div class="fig panel clearfix"><a target=_blank href="https://f1000researchdata.s3.amazonaws.com/manuscripts/24099/830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure5.gif"><img alt="830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure5.gif" src="https://f1000researchdata.s3.amazonaws.com/manuscripts/24099/830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure5.gif"></a><div class=caption><h3>Figure 5. Venn diagram showing the relationship between health literacy and the IHC framework.</h3></div></div><p class="" id=d3365e4987>The <i>GRADE framework</i> overlaps substantially with the IHC Framework with respect to critical thinking about evidence of intervention effects and decisions about what to do, as illustrated in <a href="#f6">Figure 6</a>. However, the <i>GRADE framework</i> is designed primarily for judgements by authors of systematic reviews, guideline developers, and policymakers.</p><a name=f6 class=n-a></a><div class="fig panel clearfix"><a target=_blank href="https://f1000researchdata.s3.amazonaws.com/manuscripts/24099/830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure6.gif"><img alt="830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure6.gif" src="https://f1000researchdata.s3.amazonaws.com/manuscripts/24099/830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure6.gif"></a><div class=caption><h3>Figure 6. Venn diagram showing the relationship between GRADE and the IHC framework.</h3></div></div><p class="" id=d3365e5013>Logical fallacies<sup><a href="#ref-35">35</a>,<a href="#ref-68">68</a>,<a href="#ref-76">76</a><a href="#ref-84">84</a></sup> and cognitive biases<sup><a href="#ref-36">36</a>,<a href="#ref-88">88</a><a href="#ref-96">96</a></sup> are both highly relevant to the IHC Key Concepts Framework. However, there is little similarity between the purposes of either of those types of frameworks and the purpose of the IHC Key Concepts Framework (<a href="#f7">Figure 7</a> and <a href="#f8">Figure 8</a>). Recognising the use of faulty reasoning in the construction of an argument overlaps with recognising faulty logic underlying claims about effects. However, most logical fallacies are not directly relevant to this. Similarly, recognising systematic patterns of deviation from rational judgements (cognitive biases) overlaps with judgements about effects and choices, but most cognitive biases are not directly relevant. In addition, most of the IHC Key Concepts are not logical fallacies or cognitive biases.</p><a name=f7 class=n-a></a><div class="fig panel clearfix"><a target=_blank href="https://f1000researchdata.s3.amazonaws.com/manuscripts/24099/830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure7.gif"><img alt="830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure7.gif" src="https://f1000researchdata.s3.amazonaws.com/manuscripts/24099/830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure7.gif"></a><div class=caption><h3>Figure 7. Venn diagram showing the relationship between logical fallacies frameworks and the IHC framework.</h3></div></div><a name=f8 class=n-a></a><div class="fig panel clearfix"><a target=_blank href="https://f1000researchdata.s3.amazonaws.com/manuscripts/24099/830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure8.gif"><img alt="830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure8.gif" src="https://f1000researchdata.s3.amazonaws.com/manuscripts/24099/830bc12c-700a-4d97-97cd-08b073bf894f_figure8.gif"></a><div class=caption><h3>Figure 8. Venn diagram showing the relationship between cognitive biases frameworks and the IHC framework.</h3></div></div><p class="" id=d3365e5074>There was at most some overlap between the concepts, competences, and dispositions in the included frameworks and those in the IHC Key Concepts Framework (<a href="#T8">Table 8</a>). In seven of the 16 frameworks that included concepts, there was some overlap with the IHC Key Concepts Framework. Of the 13 frameworks that included competences, there was some overlap with the IHC Key Concepts Framework in five. There was very little overlap with the dispositions included in eight frameworks.</p></div><div class=section><a name=d3365e5081 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Development of the frameworks</h3><p class="" id=d3365e5086>The methods used to develop the frameworks were clearly described for only 10 of the 22 included frameworks, and the basis was clear for only six (<a href="#T9">Table 9</a>). In total, 11 of the 22 were based in part on another framework, three on a model or theory, four on a systematic review, nine on an unsystematic review, three on a formal consensus process, and seven on an informal consensus process. The <i>evidence-based practice core competences</i> and <i>Cochrane Risk of Bias Tool</i> were the most systematically developed frameworks. Both were based in part on systematic and unsystematic reviews. The <i>evidence-based practice core competences</i> used a formal consensus process, whereas the <i>Cochrane Risk of Bias Tool</i> used an informal process.</p><a name=T9 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 9. class=n-a></a><p id=fn9> * More than one framework was considered.</p><p id=fn10><sup></sup> Although more than one framework was considered, the assessment applies to this specific framework</p><p id=fn11><sup></sup> Yes or yes for some for clear methods; yes for other bases</p></div></div></div></div><div class=section><a name=d3365e6106 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Evaluations of the frameworks</h3><p class="" id=d3365e6111>Key findings of formal and informal evaluations of the included frameworks are summarised in <a href="#T10">Table 10</a>. We found formal evaluations of seven of the 22 included frameworks. Methods used to formally evaluate the frameworks included factor analysis<sup><a href="#ref-143">143</a>,<a href="#ref-146">146</a>,<a href="#ref-187">187</a></sup>; extensive feedback (including online surveys)<sup><a href="#ref-32">32</a></sup>; principal components and Rasch analysis<sup><a href="#ref-143">143</a></sup>; systematic reviews<sup><a href="#ref-24">24</a>,<a href="#ref-29">29</a>,<a href="#ref-157">157</a>,<a href="#ref-175">175</a>,<a href="#ref-181">181</a>,<a href="#ref-185">185</a>,<a href="#ref-188">188</a></sup>; an agreement study<sup><a href="#ref-189">189</a></sup> and an assessment of the effect of training on reliability<sup><a href="#ref-182">182</a></sup>; and an assessment of usability using focus groups and online surveys<sup><a href="#ref-183">183</a></sup>. Two frameworks were evaluated both formally and informally, were found to be useful, and are widely used: the <i>GRADE framework</i><sup><a href="#ref-24">24</a>,<a href="#ref-157">157</a>,<a href="#ref-188">188</a>,<a href="#ref-189">189</a></sup> and the <i>Cochrane Risk of Bias Tool</i><sup><a href="#ref-181">181</a><a href="#ref-183">183</a>,<a href="#ref-184">184</a>,<a href="#ref-185">185</a></sup>.</p><a name=T10 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 10. Key findings of formal and informal evaluations of the frameworks.</h3></div><table xmlns:ali="http://www.niso.org/schemas/ali/1.0/" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" class=article-table frame=below><a name=d3365e6212 class=n-a></a><thead><a name=d3365e6214 name=d3365e6221 class=n-a></a>Formally<br class=br>evaluated</th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6226 class=n-a></a>Key findings</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6229 class=n-a></a>Informally<br class=br>evaluated</th><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6234 class=n-a></a>Findings</th></tr></thead><tbody><a name=d3365e6239 class=n-a></a><tr><a name=d3365e6241 class=n-a></a><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6243 class=n-a></a>Critical thinking</th><th colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e6246 class=n-a></a></th><th colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e6248 class=n-a></a></th><th colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e6250 class=n-a></a></th><th colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e6252 class=n-a></a></th></tr><tr><a name=d3365e6255 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6257 class=n-a></a>Taxonomy of<br class=br>critical thinking<br class=br>dispositions and<br class=br>abilities</td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6266 class=n-a></a>No</td><td colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e6269 class=n-a></a></td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6271 class=n-a></a>Yes</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6274 class=n-a></a>The underpinning values of Ennis work are those of rationality and logical<br class=br>thinking, with little attention paid to the impact of feelings on thinking.<br class=br>Elsewhere, Ennis defends critical thinking against cultural bias, whilst<br class=br>accepting that culture and context have serious implications for such an<br class=br>approach. Ennis aimed to produce a taxonomy which enables critical<br class=br>thinking to be used practically. He says that his taxonomy is simple and<br class=br>comprehensible and considers that it can be implemented successfully in<br class=br>different ways, though he acknowledges that it needs further research to<br class=br>validate detailed aspects<sup><a href="#ref-12">12</a></sup>.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e6299 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6301 class=n-a></a>Model of critical<br class=br>thinking</td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6306 class=n-a></a>No</td><td colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e6309 class=n-a></a></td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6311 class=n-a></a>Yes</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6314 class=n-a></a>Paul's lists of abilities and traits do not have any significant omissions<br class=br>when compared with those of Ennis or Perkins, Jay and Tishman. Nosich<br class=br>believes that it is because Pauls model of critical thinking is concept-<br class=br>based (as opposed to having rules, procedures or steps to follow), that<br class=br>it is effective in curriculum development. The model is extremely flexible,<br class=br>applicable to any subject matter and to any level of thinking. Pauls major<br class=br>contribution to the area of critical thinking is his idea of weak versus<br class=br>strong sense critical thinking. The latter is what Paul refers to as the ability<br class=br>to discover and contest ones own egocentric and socio-centric habits<br class=br>of thought. Paul claims that his nine traits of thought, which are moral<br class=br>commitments and intellectual virtues, transfer thinking from a selfish,<br class=br>narrow-minded foundation to a broad open-minded foundation<sup><a href="#ref-12">12</a></sup>.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e6346 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6348 class=n-a></a>List of critical<br class=br>thinking skills</td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6353 class=n-a></a>No</td><td colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e6356 class=n-a></a></td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6358 class=n-a></a>Yes</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6361 class=n-a></a>Halpern does not claim to have provided comprehensive lists of critical<br class=br>thinking skills. It is possible to identify many gaps in her lists. More than<br class=br>any author whose work we have reviewed, Halpern has endeavoured<br class=br>to translate theory and research from cognitive psychology into a form<br class=br>where it can be useful in everyday life. There is up-to-date teaching<br class=br>material to accompany the main text. She has also drawn on relevant<br class=br>sources outside psychology. Halpern is a strong believer in the application<br class=br>of rational methods in problem-solving, including the use of<br class=br>controlled experiments. She points to the need for people to learn how to learn and to<br class=br>be critically selective in responding to the barrage of information (including<br class=br>advertisements and political rhetoric) around them. She argues that<br class=br>teaching and assessing critical thinking will improve the quality of teaching<br class=br>and learning at college level and will increase social capital and economic<br class=br>competitiveness. These are pragmatic arguments, in support of which she<br class=br>cites several studies to illustrate the transferability of critical thinking skills<sup><a href="#ref-12">12</a></sup>.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e6399 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6401 class=n-a></a>Model of the good<br class=br>thinker</td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6406 class=n-a></a>No</td><td colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e6409 class=n-a></a></td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6411 class=n-a></a>Yes</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6414 class=n-a></a>When compared with other critical thinking theorists, there are some<br class=br>serious gaps in their enumeration of the qualities of a good thinker.<br class=br>Empathy, humility, respect for other points of view, clarity and integrity<br class=br>are signally absent. An examination of the<br class=br>biases suggests that there are several similarities, and possible overlap,<br class=br>among many of the biases, despite being assigned different names by<br class=br>different researchers. While some researchers have attempted to create<br class=br>classification schemes of decision biases, all the existing categorizations<br class=br>are based on subjective groupings, and none are mutually exclusive<br class=br>and exhaustive. To support further research in the fields of economics,<br class=br>psychology, and managerial decision-making, and to more effectively<br class=br>introduce these biases to the supply management discipline, the authors<br class=br>developed a taxonomy of these decision biases using a systematic,<br class=br>scientifically valid methodology which results in a classification which is<br class=br>both mutually exclusive and exhaustive. The scheme's<br class=br>lower positions are more explicitly epistemological than the upper<br class=br>positions, which shift "away from spatial-cognitive restructuring to<br class=br>emotional and aesthetic assessments". Thus, while the epistemological<br class=br>movement from dualism to relativism is clearly noted, how knowledge is<br class=br>construed beyond these positions is less well defined. Perry's work came<br class=br>under attack in the late 1970s for the limitations of generalizing from an<br class=br>elite male sample to the general population of college students. One of<br class=br>the persistent difficulties faced by those who wished to utilize the scheme<br class=br>as more than a theoretical lens has been the difficulty in operationalizing<br class=br>the scheme and in measuring change. Perry did not conduct further<br class=br>research to explore linkages between his conception of epistemological<br class=br>development and student learning, but he did speculate in later work on<br class=br>possible connections among cognitive styles, learning strategies, and<br class=br>development. "When students radically revise their notions of knowledge,<br class=br>would they not be likely to change their ways of going about getting it?"<br class=br><br class=br> Baxter Magolda attempted to explore gender-related patterns of<br class=br>epistemological development by studying both men and women<br class=br>conducted a longitudinal study of college students at one institution.<br class=br>Epistemology, as it appears to have been defined in this study, largely<br class=br>consisted of student perceptions of learning experiences.<br class=br><br class=br> It may be problematic that actual reflective judgment, noted in Stages 6<br class=br>and 7 of King and Kitcheners framework appears to have been attained by<br class=br>only a minute fraction of those interviewed and has appeared consistently<br class=br>only among advanced graduate students. Responses to the hypothetical<br class=br>problems posed in the interviews may tell us little about how student beliefs<br class=br>are aroused in actual experiences. We know little about how reflective<br class=br>judgment develops in context and just how education makes a difference<br class=br><br class=br> Kuhn appears to use a simplified three-stage representation of Perry's<br class=br>scheme and offers little information as to the empirical validation of this<br class=br>scheme, but in the connection of epstemological theories to reasoning.<br class=br>Kuhn's work seems least clear in the definition of elements that comprise<br class=br>epstemological theories.<br class=br><br class=br> Conceptually, the theoretical rationale for the four dimensions<br class=br>in Schommers framework is somewhat problematic. Measuring<br class=br>epistemological beliefs in paper-and-pencil questionnaire format is an<br class=br>attractive and expedient alternative to interviews. However, considerable<br class=br>questions remain about this approach, as well as about this particular use<br class=br>of survey methodology.<br class=br><br class=br> There are several important conceptual and methodological issues to be<br class=br>resolved in future research. We believe that one of the most important<br class=br>issues is the definition and delineation of the construct of epistemological<br class=br>beliefs and thinking<sup><a href="#ref-42">42</a></sup>.<br class=br><br class=br> </td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e6789 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6791 class=n-a></a>AIR model of<br class=br>epistemic cognition</td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6796 class=n-a></a>No</td><td colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e6799 class=n-a></a></td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6801 class=n-a></a>No</td><td colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e6804 class=n-a></a></td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e6807 class=n-a></a><th align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6809 class=n-a></a>Scientific thinking</th><th colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e6812 class=n-a></a></th><th colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e6814 class=n-a></a></th><th colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e6816 class=n-a></a></th><th colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e6818 class=n-a></a></th></tr><tr><a name=d3365e6821 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6823 class=n-a></a>PISA framework for<br class=br>scientific literacy</td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6828 class=n-a></a>No</td><td colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e6831 class=n-a></a></td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6833 class=n-a></a>Yes</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6836 class=n-a></a> The framework was reviewed by expert panels in each of the participating<br class=br>countries, but their feedback was not reported<sup><a href="#ref-123">123</a></sup>.<br class=br><br class=br> Procedural and epistemic knowledge are very important for interpreting<br class=br>claims from researchers, for instance in media reports. Just content<br class=br>knowledge is not sufficient to understand how science works. However,<br class=br>the description of knowledge is fairly theoretical and might be read as<br class=br>a syllabus for a course on the philosophy of science. I am convinced that<br class=br>this is not the intention, so the main challenge for PISA 2015 is to write test<br class=br>items which are feasible for 15-year olds at various ability levels<sup><a href="#ref-191">191</a></sup>. <br class=br><br class=br> The OECD sets out to determine scientific literacy for future adult<br class=br>life through a longitudinal international study, although this has been<br class=br>criticised, not least because its measures are through written tests and<br class=br>questionnaires, which generally show developing countries to be in poor<br class=br>shape to meet such a goal. In this paper it is suggested that retaining<br class=br>the use of scientific literacy is still appropriate, but it is necessary to relate<br class=br>scientific literacy to an appreciation of the nature of science, personal<br class=br>learning attributes including attitudes and also to the development of<br class=br>social values. For this, relevance of the learning plays a role and teaching<br class=br>materials, striving toward student enhancement of scientific literacy, need<br class=br>to consider a societal frame, introduction of conceptual science on a need<br class=br>to know basis, and to embrace the socioscientific situation that provides<br class=br>the relevance for responsible citizenship. The trend in defining scientific<br class=br>literacy is suggested as away from the short term product approach, in<br class=br>which the facts and skills are paramount, towards the inclusion of issue-<br class=br>based teaching, the need to go beyond scientific problem solving to<br class=br>encompass socioscientific decision making, and the recognition that<br class=br>scientific literacy relates to enabling citizens to effectively participate in<br class=br>the real world. The trend indicates a movement that gives less attention<br class=br>to scientific literacy being viewed as the possession of conceptual<br class=br>understanding of pure science abstract ideas and emphasises more<br class=br>the ability to make decisions related to the technological applications<br class=br>of scientific ideas or socioscientific issues facing society, these being<br class=br>recognized as crucial learning components<sup><a href="#ref-121">121</a></sup>. <br class=br><br class=br> There is a widespread critique of many aspects of PISA in academic<br class=br>articles, and from many different disciplines<sup><a href="#ref-192">192</a></sup>.<br class=br><br class=br> </td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e6936 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6938 class=n-a></a>Framework for<br class=br>K-12 science<br class=br>education</td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6945 class=n-a></a>Yes</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e6948 class=n-a></a>There was extensive feedback on a draft<sup><a href="#ref-32">32</a></sup>.
