All the approaches used to grade<br class=br>levels of evidence and the strength of recommendations<br class=br>prior to GRADE had important shortcomings<sup><a href="#ref-188">188</a></sup>. A review of<br class=br>systems for rating the quality (certainty) of evidence found<br class=br>that GRADE was unique in its comprehensive guidance,<br class=br>rigorous development, and dissemination strategy<sup><a href="#ref-157">157</a></sup>. A review<br class=br>of decision-making frameworks for coverage decisions<sup><a href="#ref-24">24</a></sup><br class=br>found that: Although no modifications to the GRADE EtD<br class=br>framework for coverage decisions appeared necessary<br class=br>to address the situation of effective but expensive and<br class=br>desirable interventions, modifications to some parts of the<br class=br>seven-construct framework - burden of disease, benefits and<br class=br>harms, values and preferences, resource implications, equity,<br class=br>acceptability, and feasibility - would increase its applicability<br class=br>in a range of political and health systems. Suggested<br class=br>modifications to the GRADE EtD framework include adding<br class=br>the consideration of limitations of the alternative technologies<br class=br>in use (as an elaboration of benefits and harms) and,<br class=br>more importantly, broadening acceptability and feasibility<br class=br>constructs to include political and health system factors.<br class=br>Findings of an agreement study suggest that trained<br class=br>individuals using the GRADE approach improves reliability<br class=br>in comparison to intuitive judgments about the QoE and that<br class=br>two individual raters can reliably assess the QoE using the<br class=br>GRADE system<sup><a href="#ref-189">189</a> </sup>.</td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e7278 class=n-a></a>Yes</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e7281 class=n-a></a>Although there have been criticisms of the GRADE approach, it is<br class=br>now the most widely used and highly cited approach. [<a target=xrefwindow href="https://www.gradeworkinggroup.org/" id=d3365e7285>https://www.</a><br class=br><a target=xrefwindow href="https://www.gradeworkinggroup.org/" id=d3365e7288>gradeworkinggroup.org/</a>]</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e7294 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e7296 class=n-a></a>Bradford Hill<br class=br>criteria</td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e7301 class=n-a></a>No</td><td colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e7304 class=n-a></a></td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e7306 class=n-a></a>Yes</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e7309 class=n-a></a> The framework does not reflect the current, more clearly articulated<br class=br>view of causal processes. Additionally, the guidelines used to evaluate<br class=br>evidence have not changed for decades, even as the causal questions<br class=br>have become more complex, beyond the original intent of this framework.<br class=br>One important limitation of the classic view of disease causation arising<br class=br>from the Hill criteria has been the lack of a formal basis for evaluating<br class=br>causal hypotheses. Only in the past several decades have investigators<br class=br>explored more formally the foundational mathematical and conceptual<br class=br>issues required for rigorous estimation of causal effects, particularly in<br class=br>circumstances where randomization of treatment assignment that ensures<br class=br>exchangeable comparison groups is unfeasible.<br class=br><br class=br> The inference about cause became the rationale for intervention, but the<br class=br>causal conclusions were not couched in the consequences of specific<br class=br>actions to reduce or eliminate cigarette smoking. And later, public health<br class=br>action was aimed at the individual smoker, rather than at the upstream<br class=br>system of cigarette manufacture, advertising, and distribution. This<br class=br>limited focus is a key characteristic of the traditional approach; causal<br class=br>determinations were made by epidemiologists and others in public health<br class=br>about various risk factors without considering the effect of a specific way of<br class=br>changing them.<br class=br><br class=br> The utility of long-used, familiar approaches for statistical analysis and<br class=br>causal inference to interpret the broad sweep of evidence on the causal<br class=br>determinants of human health is diminishing. Public health practitioners<br class=br>and researchers must understand the limitations of those<sup><a href="#ref-162">162</a></sup>.<br class=br><br class=br> They have to some extent withstood the test of time, in that they are still<br class=br>widely recognised and taught, but they are increasingly being replaced by<br class=br>GRADE (and other frameworks) and they are, in some ways, not consistent<br class=br>with GRADE<sup><a href="#ref-165">165</a></sup>.<br class=br><br class=br> </td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e7386 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e7388 class=n-a></a>Critical appraisal</td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e7391 class=n-a></a>Yes</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e7394 class=n-a></a>Katrak<sup><a href="#ref-29">29</a></sup> reported a systematic review of critical appraisal tools.<br class=br>Many published critical<br class=br>appraisal tools are available to critically<br class=br>appraise research reports. Many of the tools were reported to<br class=br>be modifications of other published tools or reflected specialty<br class=br>concerns in specific clinical or research areas, without attempts<br class=br>to justify inclusion criteria. Few of the generic critical appraisal<br class=br>tools could be usefully applied to any health research. Forty-two<br class=br>different items were extracted from the six critical appraisal<br class=br>tools that could be used to evaluate randomised and non-<br class=br>randomised studies. The majority of the critical appraisal tools<br class=br>were developed for a specific research design (87%), with most<br class=br>designed for use on randomised studies (38%). There is also<br class=br>a considerable number of critical appraisal tools for systematic<br class=br>reviews (N = 26).<br class=br><br class=br> There is a lack of information on tool development processes<br class=br>in most cases. Only 14 out of 121 instruments (12%) were<br class=br>reported as having been constructed using a specified<br class=br>empirical approach. Few critical appraisal tools had<br class=br>documented evidence of validity of their items, or reliability<br class=br>of use. Face validity was established in nine critical appraisal<br class=br>tools, seven of which were developed for use on experimental<br class=br>studies and two for systematic reviews. Intra-rater reliability<br class=br>was established for only one critical appraisal tool as part<br class=br>of its empirical development process, whereas inter-rater<br class=br>reliability was reported for two systematic review tools (for one<br class=br>of these as part of the developmental process) and seven<br class=br>experimental critical appraisal tools (for two of these as part<br class=br>of the developmental process).<br class=br><br class=br> Hyde<sup><a href="#ref-175">175</a></sup> reported a systematic review of evaluations of the<br class=br>effects of critical appraisal workshops, most of which use<br class=br>checklists. Sixteen studies met the inclusion criteria. One<br class=br>study was an RCT, 8 were non-randomised between group<br class=br>studies, and 7 were before-and-after studies. The impact of<br class=br>critical appraisal teaching on clinicians behaviour (principally<br class=br>reading behaviour) was mixed. Of the eight comparisons for<br class=br>this outcome six had major threats to validity. Most, but not<br class=br>all, of the comparisons showed benefit of critical appraisal<br class=br>teaching, two acting in the opposite direction. Critical appraisal<br class=br>teaching was seen to consistently increase skills: fourteen of<br class=br>the sixteen comparisons for this outcome showed a positive<br class=br>effect. The strength of the effect remained when self-assessed<br class=br>comparisons were removed. Five comparisons were thought<br class=br>not to be subject to major flaws: four of these indicated a benefit<br class=br>of critical appraisal teaching. The strongest and most consistent<br class=br>impact of critical appraisal teaching was seen on knowledge<br class=br>outcomes: 7 of the 12 studies showed a statistically significant<br class=br>positive effect. However, consideration of the size of the benefit<br class=br>revealed heterogeneity. There were four comparisons of the<br class=br>impact on attitudes - all were positive, but it was not possible<br class=br>to separate out real effects from a tendency for participants to<br class=br>respond in a desired manner. There were inadequate data to<br class=br>assess whether there was variation in outcome according to the<br class=br>mode of delivery of the educational intervention.<br class=br><br class=br> </td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e7525 class=n-a></a>Yes</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e7528 class=n-a></a>There is variation among available critical appraisal tools.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e7532 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e7534 class=n-a></a>Risk of bias</td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e7537 class=n-a></a>Yes</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e7540 class=n-a></a>West <i>et al.</i><sup><a href="#ref-185">185</a></sup> and Bai <i>et al.</i><sup><a href="#ref-181">181</a></sup> assessed whether instruments<br class=br>considered all or most of the elements for each domain and<br class=br>did not omit any element defined as essential. Savovic<br class=br><i>et al.</i><sup><a href="#ref-183">183</a></sup> assessed the usability of the Cochrane risk of bias<br class=br>tool by means of focus groups, online surveys and a face.to-<br class=br>face meeting. Da Costa <i>et al.</i><sup><a href="#ref-182">182</a></sup> assessed whether intensive,<br class=br>standardized training on risk of bias assessment improved<br class=br>the reliability of the Cochrane risk of bias tool. The Cochrane<br class=br>RoB tool was experienced positively by users and its reliability<br class=br>could be improved by training. There appears to be broad<br class=br>agreement regarding the criteria included in the Cochrane<br class=br>RoB tools.</td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e7590 class=n-a></a>Yes</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e7593 class=n-a></a>Zeng <i>et al.</i><sup><a href="#ref-184">184</a></sup> considered the Cochrane RoB tool the best available tool for<br class=br>randomised trials. Viswanathan <i>et al.</i><sup><a href="#ref-180">180</a></sup> provided general guidance without<br class=br>recommending a specific tool.</td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e7613 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e7615 class=n-a></a>Catalogue of<br class=br>biases</td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e7620 class=n-a></a>No</td><td colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e7623 class=n-a></a></td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e7625 class=n-a></a>No</td><td colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d3365e7628 class=n-a></a></td></tr></tbody></table></div><p class="" id=d3365e7634>Our assessment of the elements (concepts, competences or dispositions) in the 22 frameworks is summarised in <a href="#T11">Table 11</a>. Only one framework, the <i>framework for K-12 science education,</i> had clear inclusion criteria for one of three dimensions (core ideas). We judged the elements to be coherent in five frameworks, distinct in nine, and organised logically in eight. There were no inappropriate elements in seven frameworks and no missing elements in two. Overall, the <i>evidence-based reasoning framework</i><sup><a href="#ref-86">86</a></sup> was the only framework that we assessed positively for all five criteria (coherent elements, distinct elements, no inappropriate elements, no missing elements, and logical grouping of the elements). That framework is a relatively simple analytic model of arguments about scientific ideas.</p><a name=T11 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 11. <sup></sup> Although more than one framework was considered, the assessment applies to this specific framework</p><p id=TFN9> <sup></sup> Yes or yes for some for clear methods; yes for coherence, distinct, and logical grouping; no for inappropriate elements and missing elements</p><p id=TFN2> <sup></sup> Does not mix type(s) and specificity of concepts, competencies, or dispositions</p><p id=TFN3> <sup>** </sup>Included concepts, competencies, or dispositions are clearly different from each other</p><p id=TFN4> <sup></sup> Concepts, competencies, or dispositions included in the framework that should not have been</p><p id=TFN5> <sup></sup> Concepts, competencies, or dispositions not included in the framework that should have been</p><p id=TFN6> <sup></sup> Concepts, competencies, or dispositions organised in a way that makes sense</p></div></div></div></div><div class=section><a name=d3365e8568 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Use of the frameworks</h3><p class="" id=d3365e8573>Information about how the 22 frameworks have been used is summarised in <i>Extended data File 4</i>. We found evidence that most of the frameworks were being used. For four (the <i>taxonomy of concepts and critical abilities related to the evaluation of verbal arguments</i>, the <i>evidence-based reasoning framework</i>, the <i>AIR model of epistemic cognition</i>, and the <i>model for scientific thinking</i>) we found little evidence of use. Two had only been available for one or two years (the <i>evidence-based practice core competences</i> and the C<i>atalogue of Biases</i>), and we were uncertain about their use. Twelve of the frameworks appeared to be intended primarily for teachers and students, and we found learning resources based on 14 of the frameworks.</p><p class="" id=d3365e8598>Nine of the frameworks appeared to be intended primarily for researchers. One (the <i>evidence-based practice core competences</i>) appeared to be intended primarily for curriculum developers<sup><a href="#ref-26">26</a></sup>. We found at least some evidence that six other frameworks were used for curriculum development, including three of the critical thinking frameworks. We found evidence that 12 of the frameworks were used as the basis for one or more assessment tools. Other ways in which the frameworks have been used or have been proposed for use include: self-teaching; by parents, institutions, and government; by employers developing training programs; professional development; establishing norms or standards; developing ways of protecting against cognitive biases; theory development; intervention design; policy advice; and reporting standards.</p></div><div class=section><a name=d3365e8609 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Strengths and weaknesses of the frameworks</h3><p class="" id=d3365e8614>Strengths and weaknesses of each framework and ideas for further development of the IHC Key Concepts Framework are summarised in <a href="#T12">Table 12</a>. Strengths of the frameworks related to their development include international collaboration, support from international or national organisations, continued development over a long period of time, well described and systematic development, research evidence to support all of the concepts, elicitation of extensive feedback, and formal comparisons to similar frameworks. Strengths related to their usability include simplicity, a user-friendly structure for describing each concept, and wide use.</p><a name=T12 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 12. Research indicates that theorizing<br class=br>improves both performance and understanding<br class=br>of one's performance. Research further supports<br class=br>the claim that metacognitive theorizing can be<br class=br>facilitated by self-talk and peer interactions that<br class=br>focus on the process rather than the product of<br class=br>learning.<br class=br><br class=br>Consider incorporating discussion of tacit theories<br class=br>about how students decide what to believe and<br class=br>what to do, helping them to become aware of<br class=br>their tacit theories and to develop formal theories:<br class=br>Perhaps the most salient aspect of a tacit<br class=br>metacognitive theory as opposed to an explicit one<br class=br>is that an individual is not readily aware of either<br class=br>the theory itself or evidence that supports or refutes<br class=br>it. Thus, tacit theories are not readily distinguished<br class=br>from, or tested against, relevant data. To the extent<br class=br>that they remain tacit, metacognitive theories<br class=br>may be persistent even when they are false and<br class=br>maladaptive. One potential advantage of a formal<br class=br>metacognitive theory is that it allows the individual<br class=br>to make informed choices about self-regulatory<br class=br>behaviors.<sup><a href="#ref-103">103</a></sup></td></tr><tr><a name=d3365e9252 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e9254 class=n-a></a>Epistemological models</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d3365e9257 class=n-a></a>"Kuhn's contribution to the literature on epstemological<br class=br>understanding has been in the connection of<br class=br>epstemological theories to reasoning. overwhelming.</td></tr></tbody></table></div><p class="" id=d3365e9919>Weaknesses of the frameworks include unclear development methods, lack of formal evaluation, multiple frameworks with the same focus and no apparent agreement or effort to reach a consensus on an optimal framework, and complexity or many included concepts or competences.</p></div><div class=section><a name=d3365e9924 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Ideas for further development of the IHC Key Concepts Framework</h3><p class="" id=d3365e9929>We identified several ways in which the IHC Key Concepts Framework might potentially be improved (<a href="#T12">Table 12</a>). These include making the evidence that supports each IHC Key Concept explicit, including evidence of the extent to which each IHC Key Concept is not widely understood or applied; designing a website to popularise teaching and learning about, understanding of, and application of the IHC Key Concepts Framework; and developing a visual model of the IHC Key Concepts Framework.</p><p class="" id=d3365e9935>Overall, our review of the concepts, competences, and dispositions in the 22 frameworks led us to add four new concepts to the IHC Key Concepts Framework, to modify 16, and to add 10 new competences and four new dispositions<sup><a href="#ref-4">4</a></sup>.</p></div></div><div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d3365e9946>Discussion</h2><p class="" id=d3365e9949>We identified 22 frameworks that overlap with the IHC Key Concepts Framework. We found that the purpose of the IHC Key Concepts Framework is most like two frameworks for critical thinking: Ennis <i>taxonomy of critical thinking dispositions and abilities</i> and Barons <i>model of the good thinker</i>. However, in terms of concepts and competences, there was more overlap with Halperns <i>list of critical thinking skills</i>. Although the IHC framework drew on evidence-based health care frameworks, there was at most some similarity with the purposes of those frameworks and the purpose of the IHC Key Concepts Framework. There was some overlap in terms of concepts with GRADE, critical appraisal tools, and the <i>Catalogue of Bias</i>. There was overlap in terms of competences with health literacy, the <i>evidence-based practice core competences</i>, and critical appraisal tools.</p><p class="" id=d3365e9967>We found the IHC Key Concepts Framework to be central to critical thinking and evidence-based practice, both of which have broader scopes than the IHC Key Concepts Framework. An important weakness we found with these and other broad frameworks, such as those that focus on argumentation, is that they do not provide an adequate basis (concepts) for thinking critically about claims about the effects of interventions and decisions about what to do. As noted by Dewey: It would be impossible to over-estimate the educational importance of arriving at conceptions: that is, meanings that are general because applicable in a great variety of different instances in spite of their difference. They are known points of reference by which we get our bearings when we are plunged into the strange and unknown. Without this conceptualizing, nothing is gained that can be carried over to the better understanding of new experiences<sup><a href="#ref-196">196</a></sup>. The IHC Key Concepts are applicable to a great variety of claims about the effects of interventions, not just health interventions<sup><a href="#ref-7">7</a></sup>, and they are essential points of reference for deciding which claims to believe and what to do.</p><p class="" id=d3365e9978>We did not find any overlap between the IHC Key Concepts and those included in the <i>framework for K-12 science education,</i> and little overlap in the competences. That framework places little focus on applied science, practical understanding and use of science by non-scientists, and what children will remember and make use of in their daily lives. This may be the case for many national science curricula.</p><p class="" id=d3365e9984>Our review has helped us to clarify the goal of the IHC Key Concepts Framework and led us to add four new concepts, 10 new competences, and four new dispositions. In addition, we have identified ways in which we can improve the methods we use to further develop and evaluate the IHC Key Concepts Framework and make it more useful.</p><p class="" id=d3365e9988>Previous systematic and unsystematic reviews have reviewed different types of frameworks with similar purposes, including frameworks for cognitive biases<sup><a href="#ref-190">190</a></sup>, epistemic cognition<sup><a href="#ref-42">42</a></sup>, health literacy<sup><a href="#ref-22">22</a></sup>, assessments of the certainty of evidence and recommendations or decisions<sup><a href="#ref-24">24</a>,<a href="#ref-157">157</a>,<a href="#ref-193">193</a></sup>, causal inference<sup><a href="#ref-162">162</a></sup>, critical appraisal<sup><a href="#ref-29">29</a></sup>, and assessment of the risk of bias<sup><a href="#ref-181">181</a>,<a href="#ref-185">185</a></sup>. Moseley and colleagues<sup><a href="#ref-12">12</a></sup> conducted a comprehensive review of frameworks for thinking, which overlaps with and informed our review. However, we are unaware of other reviews with the same scope as this review, whether in terms of the included frameworks or the data that were collected for each included framework.</p><p class="" id=d3365e10033>We used explicit inclusion criteria for frameworks and two review authors independently collected data from included frameworks using a data collection form. Both the eligibility assessments and the data collection required judgement. Although we frequently disagreed, most of our disagreements were minor and all our disagreements were easily resolved. We did not conduct an exhaustive search for relevant frameworks. There may be other frameworks that meet our inclusion criteria. It is possible that other frameworks could add to our findings, but unlikely that they would otherwise substantially change the findings of this review.</p></div><div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d3365e10040>Conclusions</h2><p class="" id=d3365e10043>As defined by Moseley and colleagues: Framework is a general term for a structure that provides support<sup><a href="#ref-12">12</a></sup>. We have systematically considered 22 frameworks that are relevant to supporting critical thinking about claims about the effects of interventions (actions), comparisons (evidence used to support those claims), and decisions about what to do. We have found that the IHC Key Concepts Framework is unique and that it can be improved by building on the ways in which other related frameworks have been developed, evaluated, and made useful. Much of what we have found can also inform the development and evaluation of other frameworks.</p></div><div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d3365e10053>Data Consider the following examples, but note that this is not an exhaustive list: </p> <div class=heading5>Examples of 'Non-Financial Competing Interests'</div> <ol class="numbered-list no-padding"> <li class=standard-padding>Within the past 4 years, you have held joint grants, published or collaborated with any of the authors of the selected paper.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You have a close personal relationship (e.g. parent, spouse, sibling, or domestic partner) with any of the authors.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You are a close professional associate of any of the authors (e.g. scientific mentor, recent student).</li> <li class=standard-padding>You work at the same institute as any of the authors.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You hope/expect to benefit (e.g. favour or employment) as a result of your submission.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You are an Editor for the journal in which the article is published.</li> </ol> <div class="heading5 padding-top">Examples of 'Financial Competing Interests'</div> <ol class="numbered-list no-padding"> <li class=standard-padding>You expect to receive, or in the past 4 years have received, any of the following from any commercial organisation that may gain financially from your submission: a salary, fees, funding, reimbursements.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You expect to receive, or in the past 4 years have received, shared grant support or other funding with any of the authors.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You hold, or are currently applying for, any patents or significant stocks/shares relating to the subject matter of the paper you are commenting on.</li> </ol> </div> <div class="f1r-article-mobile research-layout"> <span class=modal-window-close-button></span> </div> </div> </div> <div class=js-article-competing-interests-policy style="display: none;"> <h2 class="h2-title u-mt--0 u-pt--0">Competing Interests Policy</h2> <p> Provide sufficient details of any financial or non-financial competing interests to enable users to assess whether your comments might lead a reasonable person to question your impartiality. Consider the following examples, but note that this is not an exhaustive list: </p> <div class=heading5>Examples of 'Non-Financial Competing Interests'</div> <ol class="numbered-list no-padding"> <li class=standard-padding>Within the past 4 years, you have held joint grants, published or collaborated with any of the authors of the selected paper.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You have a close personal relationship (e.g. parent, spouse, sibling, or domestic partner) with any of the authors.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You are a close professional associate of any of the authors (e.g. scientific mentor, recent student).</li> <li class=standard-padding>You work at the same institute as any of the authors.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You hope/expect to benefit (e.g. favour or employment) as a result of your submission.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You are an Editor for the journal in which the article is published.</li> </ol> <div class="heading5 padding-top">Examples of 'Financial Competing Interests'</div> <ol class="numbered-list no-padding"> <li class=standard-padding>You expect to receive, or in the past 4 years have received, any of the following from any commercial organisation that may gain financially from your submission: a salary, fees, funding, reimbursements.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You expect to receive, or in the past 4 years have received, shared grant support or other funding with any of the authors.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You hold, or are currently applying for, any patents or significant stocks/shares relating to 	$(function(){
		// Note: All the setup needs to run against a name attribute and *not* the id due the clonish
		// nature of facebox...
		$("a[id=googleSignInButton]").click(function(event){
            event.preventDefault();
            $("input[id=oAuthSystem]").val("GOOGLE");
            $("form[id=oAuthForm]").submit();
        });
        $("a[id=facebookSignInButton]").click(function(event){
            event.preventDefault();
            $("input[id=oAuthSystem]").val("FACEBOOK");
            $("form[id=oAuthForm]").submit();
        });
        $("a[id=orcidSignInButton]").click(function(event){
            event.preventDefault();
            $("input[id=oAuthSystem]").val("ORCID");
            $("form[id=oAuthForm]").submit();
        });
	});
</script>

        }
    });
